The Principle of 'Ibra bi-`Umum al-Lafz in the Hadith of Bi'r Buda'ah
Explaining why the general wording is not restricted by its specific cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi usul principle of 'ibra bi-`umum al-lafz to justify that the ruling of water purity from the hadith of Bi'r Buda'ah is general and not restricted to that specific well.

Core Idea
A general wording is not restricted by its specific cause; the hadith of water purity applies to all water.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a key objection raised against the Hanafi use of the hadith of Bi'r Buda'ah. Critics argue that since the hadith was a response to a question about a particular well, its ruling should be limited to that well. The Hanafi school, however, maintains the fundamental usul principle that consideration is given to the general wording of a text, not to the specific occasion that prompted it. This lesson will unpack that principle and show how it resolves the apparent conflict with other hadiths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its wording
Locate the hadith of Bi'r Buda'ah: 'Water is pure and does not become impure by anything.' Note that the wording is general (عَامّ).
The hadith is cited in the context of the objection; the exact wording is implied.
2
Understand the objection (khusus al-sabab)
Some scholars claim that because the hadith was revealed in response to a specific question about Bi'r Buda'ah, its ruling is limited to that well. This argument relies on the principle that the specific cause can restrict the general wording.
This is the basis of the question in the passage.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle: 'ibra bi-`umum al-lafz
The Hanafi rule states that in the absence of a stronger evidence for specification, the general wording retains its generality. The specific cause (sabab) does not automatically restrict the ruling.
This is a core Hanafi usul maxim.
4
Reconcile with other hadiths using takhsis
The passage mentions other hadiths (e.g., prohibition of urinating in standing water) that seem to contradict the general ruling. These are resolved by specifying (takhsis) the second case to little water, not by abandoning the general rule.
Takhsis is made by a proof that requires it, preserving the general application where possible.
5
Conclude the generality for all water
Therefore, the ruling of water purity applies to all water, regardless of the specific incident at Bi'r Buda'ah. Water remains pure unless its color, taste, or smell changes.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال إن اللام في حديث لا يبولن أحدكم في الماء للعموم حتى حرم البول في الماء القليل والكثير جميعاً فاختصت القضية الثانية بالقليل بدليل يوجب تخصيصها حتى لم يحرم ذلك الماء الذي خرج وتوضأ به جاز .اهـ. قوله : (فإن قيل العبرة لعموم اللفظ الخ) منشأ السؤال قوله فيما مر قلنا هذا ورد في بئر بضاعة الخ . قوله : (فاختصت القضية الثانية بالقليل المراد بالقضية من
And it may be said that the 'lam' in the hadith 'Let none of you urinate in water' is for generality, such that urination in both little and much water is prohibited, but the second case (the well of Buda'ah) is specified to little water by a proof that requires its specification, so that water which came out and was used for wudu is permissible. — End. His saying: ('It is said: the consideration is the generality of the wording, etc.') The source of the question is his saying earlier: 'We said this occurred in Bi'r Buda'ah, etc.' His saying: ('And the second case is specified to little water') meaning the case from...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal principle of 'ibra bi-`umum al-lafz' (consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not the specific cause). The author addresses an objection to the Hanafi application of the hadith of Bi'r Buda'ah. Critics argue that since the hadith was about a specific well, its ruling should be limited to that well. The Hanafi response is that the general wording ('الماء طهور لا ينجسه شيء') is not automatically particularized by its specific cause; rather, it remains general unless there is an equally strong text that requires specification. Here, the hadith of 'لا يبولن أحدكم في الماء الدائم' and the hadith of the sleeping person are used to reconcile apparent contradictions, but they do not negate the general ruling. The Hanafis apply the general ruling to all water, only restricting it when necessary to avoid contradiction. This is a key usul principle: a general text is not abandoned for its specific cause without a stronger counter-text.

Key Terms
العبرة بعموم اللفظ (al-'ibra bi-`umum al-lafz): The legal principle that consideration is given to the general wording of a text, not to the specific occasion of its revelation.
خصوص السبب (khusus al-sabab): The specific cause or occasion that prompted a ruling
تخصيص (takhsis): The act of restricting a general ruling to a subset of cases by a specific evidence

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the ruling of water purity from the hadith of Bi'r Buda'ah applied generally?
A) Because the well was located in Medina
B) Because the general wording 'الماء طهور' is not restricted to the specific cause
C) Because the well's water was flowing
D) Because all scholars agree on its generality

Key Takeaway
The general wording of the hadith on water purity is not restricted to its specific occasion, preserving its universal application.

Weakening of the Two-Qullah Hadith and Hanafi Water Purity
Critique of Shafi'i two-qullah hadith and defense of Hanafi water purity ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the two-qullah hadith and evaluate the strength of the Shafi'i counter-evidence.

Core Idea
The two-qullah hadith is weak; water remains pure unless its attributes change.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq engages in a critical examination of the Shafi'i hadith of two qullahs. The author systematically demonstrates its weakness through the testimony of major hadith critics and analyzes the discrepancy in its chain and text. Understanding this critique is essential for grasping why the Hanafi school does not recognise the quantity of water as a determinant of impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing evidence
What is the Shafi'i proof for the two-qullah threshold? Locate the hadith cited in the passage: 'If the water is two qullahs, it does not carry impurity.'
The hadith is narrated by the authors of the Sunan from Ibn Umar.
2
Locate the Hanafi critique
What specific weakness does the author point out? Find where he discusses discrepancy (idṭirāb) in the chain (isnād) and text (matn) of the hadith.
The author lists several critics who weakened it, including Ibn Abd al-Barr, Abu Dawud, and al-Ghazali.
3
Trace the Hanafi refutation
How does the author rebut the objection that the Shafi'i hadith should overrule the general principle? Follow the argument that the hadith is not authentic enough to override stronger evidence.
The passage mentions that the hadith is weak due to variations in narration.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the two positions? Shafi'i: water less than two qullahs becomes impure by contact even if unchanged. Hanafi: no change, no impurity.
Example: a small cup of water that remains unchanged after a drop of impurity.
5
Note the tarjih
Why is the Hanafi position relied upon (mufta bihi)? Because the opposing dalil is weak and the general principle is supported by stronger evidence like the well of Buda'ah hadith.
The tarjih authority is al-Zayla'i and other Hanafi scholars.

ينجسه شيء هذا لا تؤثر فيه النجاسة لأن السؤال إنما وقع عن ذلك والجواب إنما يقع عنه ا هـ . وقال الإمام أبو نصر البغدادي : المعروف بالأقطع لا يظن بالنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إنه كان يتوضأ من بئر هذه صفتها مع نزاهته وإيثاره الرائحة الطيبة ونهيه عن الامتخاط في الماء، فدل أن ذلك كان يفعل في الجاهلية فشك المسلمون في أمرها فبين النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه لا أثر لذلك كثرة النزح ا هـ. وقال الطحاوي : إن معنى قوله «الماء لا ينجسه شيء والله أعلم أنه لا يبقى نجساً بعد إخراج النجاسة منه بالنزح وليس هو على حال كون النجاسة فيها وإنما سألوا عنه لأنه موضع مشكل لأن حيطان البئر لم تغسل وطينها لم يخرج فبين النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أن ذلك يعفى للضرورة مثل قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم «المؤمن لا ينجس ليس معناه أنه لا يتنجس وإن اصابته النجاسة. فإن قيل : العبرة لعموم اللفظ وهو لا لخصوص السبب وهو بئر بضاعة، فكيف خص العموم بوروده في بئر بضاعة؟ قلنا : إنما لا يخص عموم اللفظ بسببه إذا لم يكن المخصص مثله في القوة وههنا قد ورد ما يخصصه وهو يساويه في القوّة وهو حديث المستيقظ، وحديث «لا يبولن أحدكم وإنما خصصناه بهذين الحديثين دفعاً للتناقض فكان من باب الحمل لدفع التناقض لا من باب التخصيص بالسبب، ولأنا ما خصصناه ببئر بضاعة بل عدينا حكمه منها إلى ما هو في معناها من الماء الجاري وترك عموم ظاهر الحديث لدفع التناقض واجب. كذا ذكره السراج الهندي وصاحب المعراج، وتعقبه في فتح القدير بأنه لا تعارض لأن حاصل النهي عن البول في الماء الدائم تنجس الماء الدائم في الجملة لا كل ماء إذ ليست اللام فيه للاستغراق للإجماع على أن الكثير لا ينجس إلا بتغيره بالنجاسة. وحاصل الماء طهور لا ينجسه شيء عدم تنجس الماء إلا بالتغير بحسب ما هو المراد المجمع عليه وإلا تعارض بين مفهومي هاتين القضيتين، وأما حديث المستيقظ من منامه فليس فيه تصريح بتنجس الماء بتقدير كون اليد نجسة بل ذلك تعليل منا للنهي المذكور وهو غير لازم أعني تعليله بتنجس الماء عيناً بتقدير نجاستهما الجواز كونه أعم من النجاسة والكراهة فنقول : نهى لتنجس الماء بتقدير كونها متنجسة بما يغير أو للكراهة بتقدير كونها بما لا يغير، وأين هو من الصريح الصحيح ؟! لكن يمكن إثبات المعارض بقوله طهور إناء إحدكم إذا ولغ فيه الكلب» الحديث فإنه يقتضي نجاسة الماء ولا يغير بالولوغ فتعين ذلك الحمل والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم ا هـ . ذلك وقد يقال إن اللام في حديث لا يبولن أحدكم في الماء للعموم حتى حرم البول في الماء القليل والكثير جميعاً فاختصت القضية الثانية بالقليل بدليل يوجب تخصيصها حتى لم يحرم ذلك الماء الذي خرج وتوضأ به جاز .اهـ. قوله : (فإن قيل العبرة لعموم اللفظ الخ) منشأ السؤال قوله فيما مر قلنا هذا ورد في بئر بضاعة الخ . قوله : (فاختصت القضية الثانية بالقليل المراد بالقضية من كتاب الطهارة ١٤٧ الاغتسال «لا في الماء الدائم الكثير مثل الغدير العظيم هكذا ذكر في معراج الدراية معزياً إلى شيخه العلامة. فعلى هذا حاصل النهي عن البول في الماء تنجس كل ماء راكد فعارض قوله ينجسه شيء وكون الإجماع أن الكثير لا يتنجس إلا بالتغير أمر آخر خارج من مفهوم الحديث وإثبات التعارض إنما هو باعتبار المفهومين. وممن صرح بأن ماء بئر بضاعة كان كثيراً الشافعي رضي الله عنه، وأما ما استدل به الشافعي فرواه أصحاب السنن الأربعة عن ابن عمر سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو يسأل عن الماء يكون في الفلاة وما ينوبه من السباع والدواب فقال : إذا كان الماء قلتين لم يحمل الخبث وأخرجه ابن خزيمة والحاكم في صحيحيهما. قلنا: هذا الحديث ضعيف وممن ضعفه الحافظ ابن عبد البر والقاضي اسماعيل ابن اسحاق وأبو بكر بن العربي المالكيون ونقل ضعفه في البدائع عن ابن المديني. وقال أبو داود: ولا يكاد يصح لواحد من الفريقين حديث عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في تقدير الماء ويلزم منه تضعيف حديث القلتين وإن كان رواه في كتابه وسكت عنه وكذا ضعفه الغزالي في الإحياء والروياني في البحر والحلية. قال في البحر هو اختياري واختار جماعة رأيتهم بخراسان والعراق ذكره النووي كما نقله عنه السراج الهندي. وقال الزيلعي المخرج: وقد جمع الشيخ تقي الدين بن دقيق العيد في كتاب الإمام طرق هذا الحديث ورواياته واختلاف ألفاظه وأطال في ذلك إطالة لخص منها تضعيفه له فلذلك أضرب عن ذكره في كتاب الإلمام مع شدة الاحتياج إليه. ووجهه أن الاضطراب وقع في سنده ومتنه ومعناه. أما الأوّل فإنه اختلف على أبي أسامة فمرة يقول عن الوليد بن كثير عن محمد بن عباد بن جعفر، ومرة عنه عن محمد بن جعفر بن الزبير ومرة يروي عن عبد الله بن عبد الله بن عمر، وقد أجاب النووي عن هذا بأنه ليس اضطراباً لأن الوليد رواه عن كل من المحمدين فحدث مرة عن أحدهما ومرة عن الآخر، ورواه أيضاً عبد الله بن عمر عن أبيهما وهما أيضاً ثبتان وأما الاضطراب في متنه ففي رواية الوليد عن محمد بن جعفر بن الزبير لم ينجسه شيء، ورواية محمد بن اسحق بسنده سئل عن الماء يكون في الفلاة فترده السباع والكلاب فقال : إذا كان الماء قلتين لا يحمل الخبث . قال البيهقي : وهو غريب. وقال اسماعيل بن عياش عن محمد بن اسحق الكلاب والدواب، ورواه يزيد بن هارون عن حماد بن سلمة فقال الحسن بن الصباح عنه عن حماد وعبيد الله ابنا عبد الله بن عمر، ومرة يروي عن الثانية تتمة حديث لا يبولن أحدكم في الماء الدائم وهي قوله ولا يغتسلن فيه من الجنابة» كما في معراج الدراية وتقدم أيضاً. قوله : (فعلى هذا حاصل النهي الخ) مراده ردّ ما قدمه عن فتح القدير أنه لا تعارض بين الحديثين بناء على تخصيصهما بالإجماع، وحاصله أن التعارض بالنظر إلى مفهوميهما مع قطع النظر عن الإجماع تأمل قوله : (أما الأول فإنه اختلف على أبي أسامة الخ) قال من
This, impurity does not affect it because the question was only about that and the answer is only about it. End quote. And Imam Abu Nasr al-Baghdadi said: The well known as al-Aqta' - it is not thought that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to perform wudu from a well of this description given his purity and preference for good scent and his prohibition of sniffling in water. So this indicated that this used to be done in the Jahiliyyah, so the Muslims were uncertain about its matter, so the Prophet (peace be upon him) explained that there is no effect of that due to the abundance of drawing. End quote. And al-Tahawi said: The meaning of his statement 'Water is not made impure by anything' - and Allah knows best that it does not remain impure after removing impurity from it by drawing, and it is not about the state when impurity is in it. They only asked about it because it was a problematic situation since the walls of the well were not washed and its clay was not removed. So the Prophet (peace be upon him) explained that that is excused due to necessity, like his statement 'The believer does not become impure' - its meaning is not that he cannot be contaminated, even if impurity befalls him. If it is said: Consideration is for the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause, and that is the well of Buda'ah. How then was the general meaning specified by its occurrence regarding the well of Buda'ah? We say: The general wording is not specified by its cause only if the specifying evidence is not equal in strength. Here, there has come what specifies it, and it is equal in strength: the hadith of the one who wakes up and the hadith 'Let none of you urinate...'. We specified it with these two hadiths to prevent contradiction, so it is a matter of interpretation to prevent contradiction, not specification by cause. Also, we did not specify it to the well of Buda'ah, but rather we extended its ruling to what is in its meaning of flowing water, and abandoning the apparent general meaning to prevent contradiction is obligatory. This is what al-Sarraj al-Hindi and the author of al-Mi'raj mentioned, and it was refuted in Fath al-Qadir by stating there is no contradiction because the gist of the prohibition of urinating in standing water is that standing water becomes impure in general, not all water, since the 'lām' is not for generality due to consensus that a large quantity only becomes impure by change. And the gist of 'Water is pure, nothing makes it impure' is that water does not become impure except by change according to the intended meaning agreed upon; otherwise there would be contradiction between the implications of these two propositions. As for the hadith of the one who wakes up from sleep, there is no explicit mention of water being impure on the assumption that the hand is impure; rather that is our reasoning for the mentioned prohibition, and it is not binding—I mean, its reasoning that water becomes impure specifically on the assumption of its impurity is possible, as it is more general than impurity and dislike. So we say: The prohibition is for the water to become impure if it is assumed to be contaminated with something that changes it, or for dislike if it is assumed to be with something that does not change it. Where is this from the explicit authentic text? But it is possible to establish the conflict with his statement 'The vessel of one of you is pure when a dog licks it' hadith, for it necessitates the impurity of water that is not changed by licking, so that interpretation is determined. And Allah the Exalted knows best. End quote. That, and it may be said that the 'lām' in the hadith 'Let none of you urinate in the water' is for generality, so it prohibits urination in both small and large quantities of water. Then the second proposition is specified to small quantities by evidence that necessitates its specification, such that it is not prohibited, and that water used for wudu is permissible. End quote. His statement: ('If it is said: consideration is for the generality of wording, etc.') The origin of the question is his earlier statement: 'We said this was revealed regarding the well of Buda'ah, etc.' His statement: ('So the second proposition is specified to small quantities') The intended proposition from the chapter of purification [page 147] is 'Do not perform ghusl in standing water that is large like a great pond'. Thus is mentioned in Mi'raj al-Dirayah, attributed to his teacher the scholar. So upon this, the gist of the prohibition of urinating in water is that every still water becomes impure, conflicting with his statement 'nothing makes it impure', and the consensus that a large quantity does not become impure except by change is something else external to the meaning of the hadith, and establishing contradiction is only based on the two implications. And among those who stated that the water of the well of Buda'ah was abundant is al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him). As for what al-Shafi'i used as evidence, it is reported by the authors of the four Sunan from Ibn Umar: 'I heard the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) being asked about water in the desert that is frequented by beasts and animals, and he said: If the water is two qullahs, it does not carry impurity.' And it was authenticated by Ibn Khuzaymah and al-Hakim in their Sahihs. We say: This hadith is weak. Among those who weakened it are al-Hafiz Ibn Abd al-Barr, al-Qadi Ismail Ibn Ishaq, and Abu Bakr Ibn al-Arabi from the Malikis. And its weakness was transmitted in al-Bada'i' from Ibn al-Madini. Abu Dawud said: 'There is hardly any authentic hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) about the measurement of water for either party, and this necessitates weakening the hadith of the two qullahs, even though he narrated it in his book and remained silent about it.' Likewise, al-Ghazali weakened it in al-Ihya, and al-Rawiyani in al-Bahr and al-Hilyah. He said in al-Bahr: 'It is my choice, and a group of scholars I saw in Khurasan and Iraq chose it,' as mentioned by al-Nawawi as quoted from al-Sarraj al-Hindi. And al-Zayla'i al-Mukharrij said: 'Sheikh Taqi al-Din Ibn Daqiq al-Eid compiled in the book al-Imam the chains of this hadith, its versions, and the differences in its wording, and he elaborated extensively, from which I summarized its weakening. Therefore, I refrained from mentioning it in the book al-Ilmam despite the great need for it. The reason is that there is discrepancy in its chain, text, and meaning. As for the first, there is disagreement about Abu Usamah: sometimes he says from al-Walid ibn Kathir from Muhammad ibn Abbad ibn Ja'far, sometimes from him from Muhammad ibn Ja'far ibn al-Zubayr, and sometimes he narrates from Abdullah ibn Abdullah ibn Umar. Al-Nawawi responded to this saying it is not a discrepancy because al-Walid narrated from each of the Muhammads, so he narrated one time from one and another time from the other, and Abdullah ibn Umar also narrated from them, and both are reliable. As for the discrepancy in its text, in al-Walid's narration from Muhammad ibn Ja'far ibn al-Zubayr: 'nothing makes it impure,' and in the narration of Muhammad ibn Ishaq with his chain: 'He was asked about water in the desert that beasts and dogs frequent, and he said: If the water is two qullahs, it does not carry impurity.' Al-Bayhaqi said: 'This is strange.' And Ismail ibn Ayyash narrated from Muhammad ibn Ishaq: 'dogs and beasts,' and Yazid ibn Harun narrated from Hammad ibn Salamah, and al-Hasan ibn al-Sabbah said from him about Hammad, and Ubaydullah the two sons of Abdullah ibn Umar, and sometimes he narrates from the second, the completion of the hadith 'Let none of you urinate in standing water,' which is 'and do not perform ghusl therein from janabah' as in Mi'raj al-Dirayah and also mentioned earlier. His statement: ('Thus, the gist of the prohibition, etc.') His intent is to refute what was presented from Fath al-Qadir that there is no contradiction between the two hadiths based on specifying them by consensus. The gist is that contradiction is considered from the perspective of their implications, disregarding consensus. Consider his statement: ('As for the first, there is disagreement about Abu Usamah, etc.') He said: 'from...'
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is an expansion and critical refinement of the ruling on water purity, specifically addressing the apparent conflict between the hadith 'الماء لا ينجسه شيء' (Water is not made impure by anything) and other ahadith that seem to indicate that water can become impure. It is an inter-madhhab dispute centered on the Shafi'i argument based on the two-qullah hadith. The author, Zayla'i in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, is defending the Hanafi position that water is only rendered impure if its color, taste, or smell changes (except for small quantities). He first cites earlier Hanafi authorities like Abu Nasr al-Baghdadi and al-Tahawi, who interpret the hadith of the well of Buda'ah as a concession due to necessity. The discussion then moves to a rebuttal of possible objections regarding the generality of the wording. The author presents the argument that the generality is specified by other equally strong hadiths (the hadith of the one waking up and the prohibition of urinating in standing water) to prevent contradiction. Then he critiques the view of Fath al-Qadir that there is no contradiction. The core of the dispute is the Shafi'i hadith of two qullahs, which the Hanafis deem weak. The author lists a number of hadith critics who weakened it, including Ibn Abd al-Barr, al-Qadi Ismail, Ibn al-Arabi, Abu Dawud, al-Ghazali, and al-Zayla'i. The weakness is due to discrepancy in chain (isnad) and text (matn). The author concludes that this hadith cannot be used to override the established Hanafi principle that water remains pure unless changed by impurity. This commentary follows the author's sequence: first, presenting the Hanafi interpretation; second, addressing the objection using the two-qullah hadith; and third, refuting that hadith as weak.

Key Terms
بئر بضاعة (Bi'r Buda'ah): A well in Medina from which the Prophet performed wudu; its water was abundant and had some impurities, yet he ruled it pure.
القلتين (al-qullatayn): Two qullahs, a measure of water volume; the Shafi'i school uses this as a threshold for impurity.
الماء الدائم (al-ma' al-da'im): Standing water, such as in a pond or well.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on water purity relies on the principle that water remains pure unless its attributes change; the Shafi'i two-qullah hadith is not accepted due to its weakness.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
12 min
Refutation of the Hadith of Qullatayn and Hanafi Position on Large Water
Critical analysis of the hadith of qullatayn and its invalidity for the Shafi'i position on water purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the hadith of qullatayn as evidence and identify the weaknesses (idtirab) in its chain, text, and meaning.

Core Idea
The hadith of qullatayn is weak due to disparity (idtirab), so the Hanafi position on water purity rests on stronger evidence.

The Shafi'i school relies on the hadith 'If the water is two qullas, it does not carry impurity' to set a volume threshold for water purity. However, Hanafi scholars have subjected this hadith to rigorous criticism, exposing contradictions that render it unreliable. This lesson walks you through the classical Hanafi critique, showing how idtirab in the chain, text, and meaning undermines the Shafi'i proof, and why the Hanafi position—that large water is pure unless changed—remains the stronger view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling on large bodies of water: they are not rendered impure unless color, taste, or smell change.
This is based on the hadith prohibiting urination in standing water and the consensus of the Companions.
2
Identify the opposing Shafi'i position
State the Shafi'i view: water less than two qullas becomes impure upon contact with impurity.
Their primary evidence is the hadith of qullatayn.
3
Examine the Shafi'i dalil
Present the hadith as cited by al-Shafi'i: 'If the water is two qullas, it does not carry impurity.'
This hadith is found in Sunan Abi Dawud, but its authenticity is contested.
4
Analyze the Hanafi critique
Break down the idtirab in the chain (different narrators), text (variant wordings), and meaning (interpretation of 'does not carry impurity').
The Hanafi response also addresses the rejection of mafhum al-shart if not accepted.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Conclude that the Hanafi position is preferred because the Shafi'i proof is weak, and many Shafi'i scholars themselves abandoned it.
This is the mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school.

الاغتسال : في الماء الدائم الكثير مثل الغدير العظيم، هكذا ذكر في معراج الدراية معزياً إلى شيخه العلامة. فعلى هذا حاصل النهي عن البول في الماء تنجس كل ماء راكد فعارض قوله ينجسه شيء وكون الإجماع أن الكثير لا يتنجس إلا بالتغير أمر آخر خارج من مفهوم الحديث وإثبات التعارض إنما هو باعتبار المفهومين. وممن صرح بأن ماء بئر بضاعة كان كثيراً الشافعي رضي الله عنه. وأما ما استدل به الشافعي فرواه أصحاب السنن الأربعة عن ابن عمر سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو يسأل عن الماء يكون في الفلاة وما ينوبه من السباع والدواب فقال: إذا كان الماء قلتين لم يحمل الخبث. وأخرجه ابن خزيمة والحاكم في صحيحيهما. قلنا: هذا الحديث ضعيف. وممن ضعفه الحافظ ابن عبد البر والقاضي إسماعيل ابن إسحاق وأبو بكر بن العربي المالكيون، ونقل ضعفه في البدائع عن ابن المديني. وقال أبو داود: ولا يكاد يصح لواحد من الفريقين حديث عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في تقدير الماء، ويلزم منه تضعيف حديث القلتين وإن كان رواه في كتابه وسكت عنه. وكذا ضعفه الغزالي في الإحياء والروياني في البحر والحلية. قال في البحر: هو اختياري واختار جماعة رأيتهم بخراسان والعراق، ذكره النووي كما نقله عنه السراج الهندي. وقال الزيلعي المخرج: وقد جمع الشيخ تقي الدين بن دقيق العيد في كتاب الإمام طرق هذا الحديث ورواياته واختلاف ألفاظه وأطال في ذلك إطالة لخص منها تضعيفه له، فلذلك أضرب عن ذكره في كتاب الإلمام مع شدة الاحتياج إليه. ووجهه أن الاضطراب وقع في سنده ومتنه ومعناه. أما الأول فإنه اختلف على أبي أسامة فمرة يقول عن الوليد بن كثير عن محمد بن عباد بن جعفر، ومرة عنه عن محمد بن جعفر بن الزبير، ومرة يروي عن عبيد الله بن عبد الله بن عمر. وقد أجاب النووي عن هذا بأنه ليس اضطراباً لأن الوليد رواه عن كل من المحمدين فحدث مرة عن أحدهما ومرة عن الآخر، ورواه أيضاً عبيد الله بن عبد الله بن عمر عن أبيهما وهما أيضاً ثبتان. وأما الاضطراب في متنه ففي رواية الوليد عن محمد بن جعفر بن الزبير: لم ينجسه شيء. ورواية محمد بن إسحاق بسنده: سئل عن الماء يكون في الفلاة فترده السباع والكلاب فقال: إذا كان الماء قلتين لا يحمل الخبث. قال البيهقي: وهو غريب. وقال إسماعيل بن عياش عن محمد بن إسحاق: الكلاب والدواب. ورواه يزيد بن هارون عن حماد بن سلمة فقال الحسن بن الصباح عنه عن حماد. ورواه عبيد الله بن عبد الله بن عمر عن أبيه: قال: دخلت مع عبيد الله بن عبد الله بن عمر بستاناً فيه مقر ماء فيه جلد بعير ميت فتوضأ منه فقلت: أتتوضأ منه وفيه جلد بعير ميت؟ فحدثني عن أبيه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: إذا بلغ الماء قلتين أو ثلاثاً لم ينجسه شيء. وروى الدارقطني وابن عدي والعقيلي في كتابه عن القاسم بإسناده إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: إذا بلغ الماء أربعين قلة فإنه لا يحمل الخبث. وضعفه الدارقطني بالقاسم. وروى بإسناد صحيح من جهة روح بن القاسم عن ابن المنكدر عن ابن عمر قال: إذا بلغ الماء أربعين قلة لم ينجس. وأخرج عن أبي هريرة من جهة بشر بن السري عن ابن لهيعة قال: إذا كان الماء قدر أربعين قلة لم يحمل خبثاً. قال الدارقطني: كذا قال وخالفه غير واحد رووه عن أبي هريرة فقالوا أربعين غرباً. ومنهم من قال أربعين دلواً. وهذا الاضطراب يوجب الضعف وإن وثقت الرجال. وأجاب النووي عن هذا الاضطراب: أما الشك في قوله قلتين أو ثلاثاً فهي رواية شاذة غير ثابتة فهي متروكة فوجودها كعدمها لكن الطحاوي أثبتها بإسناده في شرح معاني الآثار. وأما ما روي من أربعين قلة أو أربعين غرباً فغير صحيح عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وإنما نقل أربعين قلة عن ابن عمرو بن العاص، وأربعين غرباً أي دلواً عن أبي هريرة، وحديث النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم مقدم على غيره. قال النووي: وهذا ما نعتمده في الجواب. وأما الاضطراب في معناه فذكر شمس الأئمة السرخسي وتبعه في الهداية أن معنى قوله «لم يحمل خبثاً» أنه يضعف عن النجاسة فيتنجس كما يقال هو لا يحمل الكل أي لا يطيقه. وهذا مردود من وجهين ذكرهما النووي في شرح المهذب: الأول أنه ثبت في رواية صحيحة لأبي داود «إذا بلغ الماء قلتين لم ينجس» فتحمل الرواية الأخرى عليها فمعنى لم يحمل خبثاً لم ينجس، وقد قال العلماء: أحسن تفسير غريب الحديث أن يفسر بما جاء في رواية أخرى لذلك الحديث. الثاني أنه جعل القلتين حداً فلو كان كما زعم هذا القائل لكان التقييد بذلك باطلاً فإن ما دون القلتين يساوي القلتين في هذا. زاد عليه في فتح القدير وقال: هذا إن اعتبر مفهوم شرطه. وأما إن لم يعتبر مفهوم شرطه فيلزم عدم إتمام الجواب فإنه حينئذ لا يصير. قال أبو بكر بن العربي في شرح الترمذي: مداره على مطعون عليه أو مضطرب في الرواية أو موقوف. حسبك أن الشافعي رحمه الله رواه عن الوليد بن كثير وهو إباضي منسوب إلى عبد الله بن إباض من غلاة الروافض، واضطرابه في الرواية أنه روي: قلتين أو ثلاثاً، وأربعين قلة، وروي أربعون، وروي غرباً، فلا حجة علينا. ولئن صح فهو محمول على ما ذكرنا، وقد ترك جماعة من أصحابه مذهبه فيه لضعفه كالغزالي والروياني وغيرهما. كذا في معراج الدراية. قوله: (زاد عليه في فتح القدير) أي زاد وجهاً آخر على الوجهين اللذين ذكرهما النووي وهو أنه إذا لم يعتبر مفهوم الشرط يلزم عدم إتمام الجواب، وأما الوجه الأول أعني اعتبار مفهوم الشرط فهو حاصل الوجه الثاني الذي ذكره النووي.
The washing: concerning permanent abundant water like a large pond, thus mentioned in Miraj al-Dirayah attributed to his teacher the scholar. Accordingly, the gist of the prohibition of urinating in water is that every standing water becomes impure, so his statement 'something makes it impure' contradicts it, and the consensus that abundant water does not become impure except by change is another matter external to the concept of the hadith, and the establishment of contradiction is only by considering the two concepts. Among those who stated that the water of Bi'r Buda'ah was abundant was al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him. As for what al-Shafi'i used as evidence, the authors of the four Sunan narrated from Ibn Umar: I heard the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, being asked about water in the desert that beasts and animals visit, and he said: 'If the water is two qullas, it does not carry impurity.' Ibn Khuzaymah and al-Hakim also narrated it in their Sahihs. We say: This hadith is weak. Among those who weakened it are al-Hafiz Ibn Abd al-Barr, al-Qadi Isma'il ibn Ishaq, and Abu Bakr ibn al-Arabi al-Maliki; its weakness was transmitted in al-Bada'i' from Ibn al-Madini. Abu Dawud said: There is hardly any sound hadith from either party regarding the measurement of water, and its implication is to weaken the hadith of two qullas even though he narrated it in his book and remained silent about it. Al-Ghazali also weakened it in al-Ihya', and al-Ruyani in al-Bahr and al-Hilya. He said in al-Bahr: It is my choice, and a group I saw in Khurasan and Iraq chose it, as al-Nawawi mentioned, as quoted by al-Siraj al-Hindi. Al-Zayla'i al-Mukharrij said: Shaykh Taqi al-Din ibn Daqiq al-'Id collected in his book al-Imam the chains of this hadith, its narrations, and the differences in its wording, and he went on at length, concluding its weakness, so he refrained from mentioning it in his book al-Ilmam despite the great need for it. The reason is that there is disparity (idtirab) in its chain, text, and meaning. As for the first, there is disagreement about Abu Usamah: sometimes he says from al-Walid ibn Kathir from Muhammad ibn 'Abbad ibn Ja'far, sometimes from him from Muhammad ibn Ja'far ibn al-Zubayr, and sometimes he narrates from 'Ubayd Allah ibn 'Abd Allah ibn 'Umar. Al-Nawawi responded to this that it is not disparity because al-Walid narrated it from each of the two Muhammads, so he narrated once from one and once from the other, and 'Ubayd Allah ibn 'Abd Allah ibn 'Umar also narrated it from his father, and both are reliable. As for the disparity in its text, in the narration of al-Walid from Muhammad ibn Ja'far ibn al-Zubayr: 'Nothing makes it impure.' And in the narration of Muhammad ibn Ishaq with his chain: He was asked about water in the desert that beasts and dogs visit, and he said: 'If the water is two qullas, it does not carry impurity.' Al-Bayhaqi said: It is strange. Isma'il ibn 'Ayyash said from Muhammad ibn Ishaq: dogs and beasts. Yazid ibn Harun narrated it from Hammad ibn Salamah, and al-Hasan ibn al-Sabbah said from him from Hammad. 'Ubayd Allah ibn 'Abd Allah ibn 'Umar narrated from his father: He said: I entered with 'Ubayd Allah ibn 'Abd Allah ibn 'Umar a garden in which there was a pool of water containing the skin of a dead camel, and he performed ablution from it. I said: Do you perform ablution from it while it contains a dead camel's skin? He narrated to me from his father from the Prophet, peace be upon him, who said: 'If the water reaches two or three qullas, nothing makes it impure.' Al-Daraqutni, Ibn 'Adi, and al-'Uqayli narrated in his book from al-Qasim with his chain to the Prophet, peace be upon him: 'If the water reaches forty qullas, it does not carry impurity.' Al-Daraqutni weakened it due to al-Qasim. And with a sound chain from Rawh ibn al-Qasim from Ibn al-Munkadir from Ibn 'Umar: 'If the water reaches forty qullas, it does not become impure.' And a narration from Abu Hurayrah from the direction of Bishr ibn al-Sari from Ibn Lahi'ah: 'If the water is the amount of forty qullas, it does not carry impurity.' Al-Daraqutni said: Thus he said, and others disagreed, narrating from Abu Hurayrah that they said forty ghirabs, and some said forty buckets. This disparity necessitates weakness even if the men are trustworthy. Al-Nawawi responded to this disparity: As for the doubt in his saying 'two or three qullas', it is a deviant (shadhdh) narration that is not established, so it is discarded, and its existence is like its non-existence; but al-Tahawi established it with his chain in Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar. As for what was narrated of forty qullas or forty ghirabs, it is not authentic from the Prophet, peace be upon him; rather, forty qullas is reported from Ibn 'Amr ibn al-'As, and forty ghirabs, i.e., buckets, from Abu Hurayrah, and the hadith of the Prophet takes precedence over others. Al-Nawawi said: This is what we rely on in the response. As for the disparity in its meaning, Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi and, following him, al-Hidayah mentioned that the meaning of his saying 'does not carry impurity' is that it is too weak to withstand impurity, so it becomes impure, just as it is said 'he cannot carry the load,' i.e., cannot bear it. This is refuted from two aspects mentioned by al-Nawawi in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab: First, it is established in an authentic narration of Abu Dawud: 'If the water reaches two qullas, it does not become impure,' so the other narration is interpreted in light of it, and the meaning of 'does not carry impurity' is 'does not become impure'; the scholars said: The best interpretation of strange hadith is to interpret it by what comes in another narration of that hadith. Second, he made two qullas a limit; if it were as this claimant claims, then the restriction would be invalid, because what is less than two qullas would be equal to two qullas in this. Fath al-Qadir added to it and said: This is if the concept of the condition is considered. But if the concept of the condition is not considered, then the response is incomplete, and then it does not work. Abu Bakr ibn al-'Arabi said in Sharh al-Tirmidhi: Its basis is either criticized, or in a confused narration, or stopped. It is sufficient that al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, narrated it from al-Walid ibn Kathir, and he is an Ibadi attributed to 'Abd Allah ibn Ibad, one of the extremist Rafidah. His confusion in narration is that it was narrated as 'two or three qullas,' 'forty qullas,' 'forty,' and 'ghirab,' so there is no proof for us. Even if it is authentic, it is interpreted as we mentioned, and a group of his own followers abandoned their school due to its weakness, like al-Ghazali, al-Ruyani, and others. Thus in Miraj al-Dirayah. His saying: (He added to it in Fath al-Qadir) means he added another aspect to the two aspects mentioned by al-Nawawi, which is that if the concept of the condition is not considered, then the response is incomplete; and the first aspect, i.e., considering the concept of the condition, is the same as the second aspect mentioned by al-Nawawi.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute analysis. The author, commenting on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses the Hanafi ruling that large bodies of standing water (like a large pond) do not become impure merely by contact with impurity unless their color, taste, or smell changes. The Shafi'i school argues that water less than two qullas (approximately 270 liters) becomes impure upon contact, citing the hadith: 'If the water is two qullas, it does not carry impurity.' The author refutes this by first noting that the hadith is weak (da'if) according to many prominent scholars, including Ibn Abd al-Barr, al-Qadi Isma'il, Abu Bakr ibn al-'Arabi, al-Ghazali, al-Ruyani, and even Abu Dawud. He then details the contradictions (idtirab) in the hadith's chain (sanad), text (matn), and meaning (ma'na). In the chain, the narrator Abu Usamah reports from multiple sources, leading to confusion; al-Nawawi tries to resolve this, but the author implies it remains problematic. In the text, some versions say 'nothing makes it impure' while others say 'does not carry impurity'; numbers vary between two, three, and forty qullas. In meaning, al-Sarakhsi interpreted 'does not carry impurity' as 'cannot withstand impurity' (i.e., becomes impure), but the author rejects this, noting that an authentic version in Abu Dawud says 'does not become impure' and that the proper interpretation is to harmonize by taking the clearer version. The author concludes that the hadith is unreliable, and even many Shafi'is (like al-Ghazali and al-Ruyani) abandoned their school's position on this. The Hanafi position stands on the stronger evidence of the hadith prohibiting urination in standing water and the consensus that large water is not impure unless changed.

Key Terms
قلتين (qullatayn): Two qullas, a measure of volume; approximately 270 liters according to later Hanafi estimates.
الخبث (al-khabath): Impurity (najis) in the context of water; the hadith says 'does not carry khabath' meaning it does not become impure.
اضطراب (idtirab): Contradiction or inconsistency in a hadith's chain or text that renders it weak.
مفهوم الشرط (mafhum al-shart): The implied meaning of a conditional statement; here, the hadith 'if the water is two qullas, it does not carry impurity' implies that less than two qullas does carry impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason the Hanafi school rejects the hadith of qullatayn?
A) The hadith is only found in the Sunan and not in the Sahihayn.
B) The hadith has disparity (idtirab) in its chain, text, and meaning, making it weak.
C) The Hanafi school does not accept any hadith from Abu Dawud.
D) The Hanafi school only relies on the Quran for rulings on water.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects the hadith of qullatayn due to its weakness (idtirab), and accordingly does not use a volume threshold for water purity, relying instead on the principle that water is pure unless its qualities change.

Taharah — Purification
advanced
10 min
The Qullatayn Dispute: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i on Water Purity
Inter-madhhab dispute on the qullatayn hadith and its implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on water impurity, evaluate the Shafi'i reliance on the qullatayn hadith, and identify the practical difference in rulings.

Core Idea
Water becomes impure only by change, not by falling short of a specific measure.

The question of when water becomes impure is a classic point of contention between madhhabs. The Shafi'i school holds that water less than two qullas (approx. 190 liters) is automatically rendered impure by contact with impurity, while Hanafis insist that the decisive factor is whether the water changes in color, taste, or smell. This lesson examines the proofs on both sides and the Hanafi critique of the qullatayn hadith, clarifying why the Hanafi school adheres to the change-based criterion.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that water does not become impure unless it changes (color, taste, smell) due to impurity. There is no fixed quantity threshold; the criterion is the presence or absence of perceptible change.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Shafi'i school (and Ahmad ibn Hanbal) holds that if water is less than two qullas, it becomes impure regardless of change; if it is two qullas or more, it remains pure unless it changes.
This view relies on the hadith 'If water reaches two qullas, it does not carry impurity.'
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof consists of the qullatayn hadith and the concept of mafhum al-shart (implication of the condition): the ruling 'does not carry impurity' applies when the condition (reaching two qullas) is met, implying the opposite when it is not met.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis argue that the hadith is weak (da'if) and criticized by scholars such as Ibn Taymiyyah. Even if authentic, it contradicts stronger evidence like the authentic hadith on urinating in still water and rational consistency. The Hanafi school also points to the lack of early practice and the irrational consequences of the Shafi'i view.
The Hanafi rebuttal includes: (1) the hadith is weak, (2) it is abrogated by the later hadith from Abu Hurayrah, (3) its application leads to absurdities (e.g., two dirty qullas becoming pure when combined).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically: A Hanafi rules that if impurity falls into water (of any quantity) and no change occurs, the water remains pure. A Shafi'i rules that if the water is less than two qullas, it becomes impure even without change; only at or above two qullas does the change criterion apply.
Example: A drop of urine falls into 50 liters of water with no perceptible change. Hanafi: pure. Shafi'i: impure.

Two qullas means it is weak to benefit its ruling if it exceeds two qullas, and the question is about that water in any case. An-Nawawi only confined himself to what he mentioned because he says that the concept of the condition is a proof. But al-Khabbazi said: The meaning of his statement 'If the water reaches' is a decrease, not an increase. If it is said: What is above two qullas as long as it does not reach ten by ten is also probable of impurity, so what is the benefit in specifying it with two qullas? It is said to him: It is possible that it was revealed to him that a mujtahid will come and say that water, if it reaches two qullas, does not bear impurity, so the Prophet, peace be upon him, said in reply to that statement. End. And it is, as you see, extremely far. The researcher in Fath al-Qadir said: The relied-upon is the confusion in the meaning of qulla, for it is polysemous, applied to jar, water skin, and mountain top. And what al-Shafi'i interpreted it with is disconnected due to ignorance, for he said in his Musnad: Muslim ibn Khalid al-Zanji informed me from Ibn Jurayj with an isnad that I do not recall that he said: If water is two qullas, it does not carry impurity. And he said in the hadith: 'With qilal of Hajar.' Ibn Jurayj said: I saw the qilal of Hajar; the qulla holds two water skins or two and a bit. Al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: So the precaution is to make it two and a half water skins, so if it is five large water skins like the water skins of the Hijaz, it does not become impure unless it changes. And Hajar, with fath of ha and jim, is a village near Medina. So it is established by this that the hadith is derived from... And he did not mention the second aspect that he mentioned in al-Fath because an-Nawawi says that the concept of the condition is authoritative. This speech is like this, and it contains discussion, because the concept of the condition applies to what is above two qullas, not to what is below them, as is the basis of an-Nawawi's second objection. For what is below them becomes impure by textual indication as in His statement: 'So do not say to them uff' [al-Isra: 23]. So if that which is two qullas becomes impure, then it is even more so that what is below them becomes impure. So it is not included under the concept of the condition; rather, what is included is the excess over them, meaning it is understood that what is more does not become impure. So it is not appropriate for the Hanafi to interpret it with the mentioned meaning, namely that it is weak in the face of impurity, since he does not say that what exceeds two qullas is not impure as long as it is not a ghadir. And this, as you see, is other than what an-Nawawi mentioned. So his statement 'This, if his concept of condition is considered' is an indication that what an-Nawawi mentioned is incorrect. So he should have explained it as an independent aspect. There is no harm in mentioning the phrase of al-Fath to clarify what we have said. So we say: He said in al-Fath, objecting to what is in al-Hidayah: This necessitates one of two things: either the answer is not complete if his concept of condition is not considered, for then it does not benefit its ruling if it exceeds two qullas, and the question is about that water in any case; or the concept is considered to complete the answer, and the meaning then is that if it is two qullas it becomes impure, not if it exceeds. So if its consideration here is necessary due to the proof established for it, which is so that the question is not left without a corresponding answer, then what is established by it would be contrary to the school, for we did not say that if it exceeds two qullas by something it does not become impure unless it changes. End. And by this you also know that the objection based on considering the concept of condition is not built on the claim of its absolute authority, but rather on its consideration here due to a proof established for it, as you have learned, so be insightful. His statement: 'But al-Khabbazi said, etc.' means that water, if it was much then decreased and became two qullas, becomes weak in bearing impurity, so it becomes impure. And you know that there is nothing in the previous speech that could be an objection to it. Yes, it could be an objection to what the author of al-Fath rejected in the second aspect that occurred in his speech, that it necessitates that what is established by it is contrary to the school, and what is used for the objection is derived from his statement 'If it is said, etc.' And its summary is that the hadith of two qullas is weak. For if you say: Ibn Majah, Ibn Khuzaymah, al-Hakim, and a group of hadith scholars have declared it authentic, I say: Those who declared it authentic relied on some of its chains and did not look at its wording and its implication, for this is not the function of a muhaddith; looking into that is the function of a faqih, since his purpose after authentic transmission is fatwa and acting on the meaning. And the Hafiz, the scholar of the Arabs, Abu al-Abbas ibn Taymiyyah, went to great lengths in declaring it weak and said: It is likely that al-Walid ibn Kathir made a mistake in raising the hadith and attributing it to Ibn Umar, for he constantly gives fatwa to people and narrates from the Prophet, and what is narrated is known among the people of Medina and others, especially from his son Salim and his freedman Nafi', and neither Salim nor Nafi' narrated this from him, nor did any scholar of Medina act on it. And he mentioned from the Tabi'un what contradicts this hadith, then he said: How can this be the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, with the general need for it, and no one among the Companions or the Tabi'un who followed them in goodness narrated it except a different, confused narration from Ibn Umar, and no one from the people of Medina, Basra, Syria, or Kufa acted on it? And he, may Allah have mercy on him, spoke at length beyond what this place can bear. And what al-Daraqutni narrated from Salim from his father does not harm the Hafiz due to its weakness. And an-Nawawi's statement that its limit is what the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, limited, which Allah made obligatory and forbade violating, and their limit—meaning the Hanafis—is contrary to his limit, while he limited it with something that has no basis and no precision, is rebutted by the fact that what you have used as proof is weak as previously mentioned, and what we have adopted is supported by shari'ah and reason. As for shari'ah, we have presented the hadiths reported about that. As for reason, we are certain that impurity does not reach the other side, or it is the predominant assumption, and assumption is like certainty. So we have used water that has no certainty of impurity, and Abu Hanifah did not estimate that with anything but rather considered the predominant assumption of the mukallaf. So this is a rational proof supported by the authentic hadiths mentioned, so acting on it is necessary. And because our proof, which is the hadith prohibiting urinating in still water, is authentically reported in the Sahihain from the narration of Abu Hurayrah, and his embracing Islam was later, and the hadith of two qullas is the hadith of Ibn Umar, and his embracing Islam was earlier, and the later abrogates the earlier if it were established. And al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: If the change of two qullas disappears by itself, the water becomes purified while the urine, feces, and other impurities remain, so then the impurity of urine, feces, and wine is based on smell, color, and taste, not on their essence. And this is not reasonable nor supported by the principles of shari'ah. And if a dirty qulla is added to a dirty qulla, they both become pure according to them. This leads to pure water becoming impure with a little impurity but not with a lot, because they deem a pure qulla impure with a ratl of dirty water but do not deem it impure with a dirty qulla of water; rather, they purify it with it. And it also leads to the generation of pure from the combination of two impurities, which reason deems impossible. His statement: 'Otherwise it is like flowing water' means if it is not ten by ten, then it is like flowing water, so it does not become impure. But that which exceeds two qullas does not lead to the objection that it would not be impure, since the specification of impurity to two qullas is for the benefit of replying to the one who will say there is no impurity, and that which led the commentator into this...
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the hadith of qullatayn and its implications for water purity. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is discussing a point from al-Hidayah and the objections raised against the Hanafi position. The core issue is whether the measure of two qullahs (qullatayn) is a decisive threshold for water becoming impure. The Shafi'i school, following an-Nawawi, holds that water reaching two qullahs cannot become impure unless it changes, based on the hadith and the concept of mafhum al-shart (implication of the condition). The Hanafi school, however, does not accept this as a strict limit. Instead, Hanafis consider the absence of change (taghayyur) as the criterion: water is pure unless its color, taste, or smell changes due to impurity. The passage clarifies that the Shafi'i reliance on the hadith is problematic because the hadith is weak. It cites critiques from Ibn Taymiyyah and others, and also points out contradictions in the Shafi'i position, such as the idea that a dirty qulla added to another dirty qulla becomes pure, leading to irrational consequences. The Hanafi position is defended by rational evidence: if there is no certainty that impurity reached the other side, the water remains pure based on predominant assumption (ghalabat al-zann). The passage also mentions that Abu Hanifah did not fix a specific measure but relied on the mukallaf's predominant assumption. Furthermore, the Hanafi proof from the hadith prohibiting urinating in still water (which is sahih) is considered stronger and, if established, would abrogate the weaker qullatayn hadith. The author concludes that the Hanafi view is supported by both shari'ah and reason.

Key Terms
القلتان (al-qullatān): A measure of water used in hadith; two qullas (sing. qulla) equal to approximately 160-190 liters depending on interpretation. In this text, it refers to the specific quantity mentioned in the hadith.
مفهوم الشرط (mafhūm al-sharṭ): The legal implication of a conditional statement: that the ruling mentioned applies only when the condition is present, and the opposite ruling applies when it is absent.
الغدير (al-ghadīr): A large pool or pond of water that is not flowing; in Hanafi fiqh, if water is a large ghadir, it is considered like flowing water and does not become impure unless it changes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for the Hanafi school's position on water impurity?
A) The hadith of qullatayn.
B) The principle of change (taghayyur) in color, taste, or smell.
C) The concept of mafhum al-shart.
D) The consensus of the Sahaba.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely holds that water remains pure unless it undergoes a perceptible change due to impurity, rejecting any fixed quantity as a threshold.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
10 min
Water >2 Qullas: Does the Spot of Impurity Become Impure?
Intra-Hanafi expansion on the spot of impurity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on the spot of impurity in water exceeding two qullas and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
The spot of impurity in large water is disputed: Abu Hanifa says it becomes impure; fatwa says it does not, due to general need.

Water exceeding two qullas (approx. 200 L) is generally treated like flowing water: it remains pure unless one of its three properties changes. However, a key intra-Hanafi dispute arises: does the exact spot where an impurity falls itself become impure? This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq expands on this question, presenting the arguments of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and later scholars, and concludes with the mufta bihi position that facilitates daily practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
First, note that water exceeding two qullas is like flowing water: it does not become impure unless one of its three properties (colour, taste, smell) changes. This is the foundational ruling from which the dispute on the spot arises.
This ruling is agreed upon within the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the dispute
The passage then asks: does the exact spot where the impurity falls become impure? Identify the two main positions: Abu Hanifa holds it becomes impure; Abu Yusuf and the scholars of Bukhara hold it does not.
The dispute is only about the spot, not about the water as a whole.
3
Examine the evidence for each view
Abu Hanifa argues from certainty: the impurity is definitely at that spot, so it is impure. Abu Yusuf analogizes to flowing water: since the abundance of water prevents impurity from settling, the spot remains pure.
The passage also distinguishes visible vs. non-visible impurities.
4
Determine the tarjih (preferred view)
The passage indicates the mufta bihi view: the spot does not become impure, especially for non-visible impurities, due to general need (umum al-balwa). This is the view chosen by the scholars of Bukhara and endorsed in later works like Fath al-Qadir.
This is the operative fatwa today.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: according to Abu Hanifa, one must leave a small tank's distance (approx. 4×4 cubits) from the spot; according to Abu Yusuf, one may use the spot directly. The fatwa follows Abu Yusuf.
For non-visible impurities, the spot is considered pure even without stirring.

His statement: (otherwise it is like flowing water) i.e. if it is ten by ten, it is like flowing water, so it does not become impure except if one of its descriptions changes. Then in his statement 'like flowing water' there is an indication that the spot where the impurity falls does not become impure, and this is narrated from Abu Yusuf, and the scholars of Bukhara adopted it, and it is the chosen position according to them, as stated in al-Tabyin. And in Fath al-Qadir: it is the one that should be corrected, so there should be no difference between visible and non-visible, because the evidence only requires with abundance of water non-impurity except with change without distinction, and it is also the consensus ruling and in al-Nisab, and upon it is the fatwa as in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli. And it was corrected in al-Mabsut and al-Mufid that the spot of occurrence becomes impure, and al-Quduri indicated this in his statement: 'it is permissible to perform ablution from the other side.' And Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi mentioned that anything mixed with impurity is not permissible to perform ablution with, even if it is flowing, and this is the correct view. Al-Zayla'i said: according to this, what the author mentioned does not indicate that the spot of occurrence does not become impure, because he only made it like flowing water, so if the spot of occurrence becomes impure in flowing water, then it is more appropriate that it becomes impure here. And in al-Bada'i': the apparent narration is that one does not perform ablution from the side where the impurity fell, but performs ablution from the other side, meaning one leaves from the spot of impurity the size of a small tank then performs ablution. This is how it was interpreted in al-Imla' from Abu Hanifa, because we are certain of impurity on that side and doubt about beyond it. And upon this, they said about one who cleans himself in a spot of a tank: it does not suffice him to perform ablution from that spot before moving the water. And if a carcass falls in the middle of a tank, according to the apparent narration, if between the carcass and each side of the tank there is an amount that prevents some from reaching some, it is permissible to perform ablution there, otherwise not. And if it is non-visible, such as a person urinating or a junub bathing, the mashayikh differed about it. The Iraqi scholars said: its ruling is the ruling of the visible, so one does not perform ablution from that side, unlike flowing water. And our scholars from beyond the river distinguished between them concerning non-visible: that one performs ablution from whichever side, as they all said about flowing water, and this is most correct, because non-visible does not settle in one place but moves due to being liquid and flowing by nature, so one is not certain of impurity on the side from which he performs ablution, unlike the visible. This is also the view of Qadikhan: one leaves from the spot of impurity the size of a small tank. And the size of a small tank in al-Kifaya Sharh al-Hidaya is four cubits by four. And in al-Dhakhira from some of them: one stirs the water with his hand to the extent needed for ablution; if the impurity moves, one does not use that spot. And some said: one should make a judgment; if his judgment is that the impurity did not reach that spot, he may perform ablution and drink from it. It is said in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli that this is the most correct. And in Mi'raj al-Dirayah attributed to al-Mujtaba that the fatwa is on the permissibility of ablution from the spot of occurrence, and the scholars of Bukhara chose it due to general need, so they said: it is permissible to perform ablution from the spot of cleaning before stirring.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq is an expansion on the ruling of water exceeding two qullas (approximately 200 liters). The core ruling is that such water is treated like flowing water: it does not become impure unless one of its three properties (color, taste, smell) changes. The commentary then addresses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: does the exact spot where an impurity (like urine or a carcass) falls itself become impure? According to a narration from Abu Yusuf and the view adopted by the scholars of Bukhara, the spot does not become impure, because the abundance of water means it is like flowing water, where the spot is not considered impure. This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) view, as stated in Fath al-Qadir and other works, due to general need (umum al-balwa). In contrast, the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) from Abu Hanifa holds that the spot does become impure, so one should avoid that side and perform ablution from another side, leaving a distance equivalent to a small tank (about 4×4 cubits). The passage further distinguishes between visible impurities (like a carcass) and non-visible ones (like someone urinating or a junub bathing). For visible, the dispute remains. For non-visible, the scholars of Transoxiana (ma wara' al-nahr) rule that since such impurities mix and do not settle, one may use any side, which is deemed the strongest view. Thus, the practical fatwa today, especially due to common need, permits ablution even from the spot of cleaning before stirring the water, as chosen by the Bukhara scholars.

Key Terms
القلتين (al-qullatayn): Two qullas; a measure of water volume, approximately 200 liters. Water less than this becomes impure by mere contact with impurity; water more than this does not unless it changes.
الجاري (al-jari): Flowing water; it does not become impure by mere contact unless one of its three properties changes.
موضع الوقوع (mawdi' al-wuqu'): The exact spot where a visible or non-visible impurity falls into water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on performing ablution from the exact spot where a non-visible impurity (like urine) fell in a large tank of water exceeding two qullas?
A) It is not permissible at all; one must use another side.
B) It is permissible even without stirring the water, due to general need.
C) It is permissible only after moving the water by hand.
D) It is impermissible until the water is changed.

Key Takeaway
In water exceeding two qullas, the spot of a non-visible impurity does not become impure per the mufta bihi view, allowing its use due to general need.

Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on the Spot of Najasah in Water
Intra-Hanafi dispute on impurity of the spot of fall

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the different Hanafi positions on whether the place of najasah fall becomes impure and apply the tarjih based on the type of impurity.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school is not monolithic: the spot of najasah fall may be considered impure or pure depending on scholar and type of impurity.

In this lesson, we dive into an intra-Hanafi dispute over a practical question: when najasah falls into water, does the exact spot become impure? The passage presents multiple opinions from early authorities like Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and al-Karkhi, as well as later tarjih. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for applying the correct ruling in daily worship, especially when dealing with visible versus invisible impurities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The core question is whether the spot where najasah falls (mawdi' al-wuqu') in flowing or still water itself becomes impure, affecting the validity of wudu from that spot.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school; external schools may have different approaches.
2
State the opinions of major Hanafi authorities
List the positions: Abu Yusuf and Bukhari mashayikh say the place does not become impure; Abu Hanifa (zahir al-riwaya) says it does; al-Karkhi says any mixing renders water impermissible; later scholars favor permissibility due to common calamity.
The passage also cites al-Zayla'i who argues that if flowing water's spot becomes impure, then still water's spot is more likely impure.
3
Distinguish between visible and invisible impurities
For visible impurities (carcass, visible urine), certainty of contamination is higher; for invisible (urine, ghusl water), impurity spreads and certainty is lacking. The Iraq mashayikh treat both alike, while Transoxiana scholars differentiate.
This distinction is key to the fatwa.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
The later fatwa, as in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli and al-Mujtaba, permits wudu from the place of fall, especially for invisible impurities, due to umum al-balwa (common calamity).
This overrules the stricter positions for practical necessity.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a person performing istinja in a tank: according to the fatwa, they may use the same spot for wudu without moving water, provided the impurity is not visible.
If visible, they should leave the area of a small tank (4x4 cubits) as a precaution.

Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute over whether the exact spot where najasah falls in flowing water becomes impure. The author presents multiple positions. First, Abu Yusuf is reported to hold that the place of fall does not become impure, and this was adopted by the mashayikh of Bukhara, and it is considered the chosen view in al-Tabyin. Fath al-Qadir agrees, extending this to both visible and invisible impurities, arguing that the principle only requires changing of water for impurity, and this is the consensus. However, in al-Mabsut and al-Mufid, the position is that the place does become impure, and this is what al-Quduri implied by saying one may only use the other side. Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi strongly states that any water mixed with impurity is prohibited regardless of flow. Al-Zayla'i points out that the author's text does not negate impurity at the spot, because by equating it to flowing water, if the spot in flowing water becomes impure, then it is even more so here. Then from al-Bada'i', the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) is that one cannot use the side where impurity fell, but can use the other side, leaving a small tank's area (4×4 cubits). This is from Abu Hanifa based on certainty vs. doubt. For invisible impurities (like urine), the mashayikh of Iraq treat it like visible, while those beyond the river distinguish because invisible does not remain fixed but spreads, making certainty impossible. Qadikhan and others give practical concessions, with some allowing wudu from the spot of istinja' before moving water due to common need. The passage concludes with a note that the fatwa is on the permissibility of using the place of fall, especially for necessity.

Key Terms
موضع الوقوع (mawḍiʿ al-wuqūʿ): The exact spot where najasah falls into water.
المرئية (al-marʾiyya): Visible impurity, like a carcass or visible urine.
غير المرئية (ghayr al-marʾiyya): Invisible impurity, like urine or ghusl water from a junub that is not visible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the passage, which of the following is true for wudu from the spot where an invisible impurity (e.g., urine) fell in still water?
A) It is prohibited because the spot is impure.
B) It is permissible because there is no certainty of contamination.
C) It is obligatory to use only the other side.
D) It is makruh tanzihi.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits wudu from the spot of najasah fall for invisible impurities due to necessity and lack of certainty; for visible, precaution recommends leaving the area.

Defining Flowing Water and the Trace of Impurity
Definition of flowing water and ruling on trace of impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of flowing water, explain the condition of trace (athar) for impurity, and apply the ruling to determine whether wudu is valid.

Core Idea
Flowing water remains pure unless taste, color, or odor of impurity appears.

The passage introduces the classical Hanafi definition of flowing water as water that moves a straw by its flow. It then clarifies the critical rule: such water is pure for wudu unless a trace of impurity – taste, color, or odor – becomes evident. This trace is the sole criterion for impurity, settling a common misunderstanding that mere contact with najasah renders flowing water impure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition
Find the linguistic and legal definition of flowing water given in the text. Note the phrase 'ma yadhabu bi-tibna' and understand why the author uses a straw as a measure.
The definition is based on common understanding, not a fixed measurement.
2
Understand the Trace Criterion
The passage lists three types of trace: taste, color, odor. Recognize that these are the only signs that make water impure; mere mixing without trace does not affect purity.
Trace must be perceptible; if the impurity is unseen and no change is detected, water remains pure.
3
Apply the Rule to Scenarios
Practice applying the rule: if a drop of blood falls into a flowing river, does it become impure? Only if its taste, color, or odor spreads. Otherwise, it remains pure.
This differs from still water, which has quantity limits (10x10 cubits).
4
Refute the Grammatical Objection
The commentary addresses an objection that a camel or ship also moves straws, which would invalidate the definition. Understand the resolution: 'ma' here is an indefinite noun described, not a relative pronoun.
The definition is metaphorical: 'water that, by its flow, can move a straw' – not that it actually moves one.
5
Note the Intra-Hanafi Nuance
Al-Zayla'i mentions a narration that still water of 10x10 cubits is treated like flowing water at the place of fall. Recognize that the chosen view is that it remains permissible if unchanged.
This shows how the ruling extends to similar cases.

أثر النجس كيف يكون الصحيح عدم جواز الوضوء به، وحينئذ فلا ينبغي ذكره هنا لأن المراد ما إذا لم يظهر أثر النجاسة، وبه يعلم ما في كلام الزيلعي فتدبر، ثم رأيت في الشرنبلالية ذكر ما قلته والله الحمد. قوله: Book of Purification: And it (flowing water) is what moves a straw. And one performs wudu from it if he does not see its trace, and it is taste, color, or odor, and the death of what has no blood. His saying: (And it is what moves a straw) meaning flowing water moves a straw. And some students erroneously thought this definition is invalid because a camel and a ship are countered against it, for they move many straws. The source of the error is that 'ma' is a relative pronoun in his speech, and a similar occurrence happened in Ibn al-Hajib's statement: 'Speech is what comprises two words with attribution.' So it was said that a leaf and a stone with two or more words written on them are countered because 'ma' is a relative pronoun meaning 'that which'. But the answer to both is that 'ma' is not a relative pronoun but rather an indefinite noun described. So the meaning is: flowing water is water that, by its flow, moves a straw, and speech is an expression comprising two words. And there is disagreement on the definition of flowing water; among the sayings is what the author mentioned, and the soundest is what people consider flowing, as mentioned in al-Bada'i' and al-Tabyin and many books. His saying: (So he performs wudu from it) i.e., from flowing water. Al-Zayla'i said: It is permissible that it refers to still water that reaches ten by ten, because it is permissible to perform wudu from it at the place of fall as long as it has not changed, in one narration, and it is the chosen view of them. His saying: (If he does not see its trace) meaning if he does not know the trace of the impurity in it. And 'ra'a' is used in the sense of 'knowledge'. The poet said: 'I saw God greater than everything.' We only say this because taste and smell are not perceived by sight; rather, taste is by tasting and smell by smelling. His saying: (And it is taste, color, or odor) meaning the trace is what was mentioned. The summary is that flowing water and what is in its ruling, if impurity falls into it, if its trace appears, it is not permissible to perform wudu with it; otherwise it is permissible, because the existence of the trace is evidence of the existence of impurity. So everything in which we are certain of impurity or that is our predominant opinion is not permissible to perform wudu with, whether flowing or otherwise, because flowing water is not rendered impure by the fall of impurity, as might be erroneously imagined. And what is apparent from the texts is that flowing water, if impurity falls into it, it is permissible to perform wudu with it if its trace is not seen, whether the impurity is a visible carcass or it contains impurity by its trace appearing, not mere mixing, as evidence by his saying 'even if flowing.' For if it is flowing and the trace of impurity does not appear, how could the correct (opinion) be the impermissibility of wudu with it? Therefore, it should not be mentioned here because the intended meaning is when the trace of impurity does not appear. And by this, what is in al-Zayla'i's statement is known, so reflect. Then I saw in al-Shurnubalaliyya what I said, and praise be to God. His saying: (And some students erroneously thought, etc.) It is said: He is al-Rumi, the sheikh of the Ashrafiyya school; he raised the objection and was laughed at. And al-Sheikh Qasim mentioned it in his gathering as a mockery of its speaker. The wonder is not at him, but rather at the commentator for mentioning it here. His saying: (But the answer to both is that 'ma' is not a relative pronoun but rather an indefinite noun described.) I say: The indefinite noun described, which is estimated as 'something' as mentioned by Ibn Hisham in Mughni al-Labib. So what was objected regarding its being a relative pronoun also applies to its being described, with even more reason. The truth is that the pronoun refers to the flowing water mentioned before, so the relative pronoun is an adjective for it. And it is permissible to estimate it as an indefinite noun described, but with the specification of the word 'something', i.e., flowing water is something of water that moves a straw. It is not hidden that the first is more appropriate and that the objection is invalid from its root. His saying: (And it is permissible that it refers to still water) I say: this is...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling. It begins by defining flowing water linguistically as water that moves a straw (a light object). The commentary refutes an objection that a camel or ship would also move a straw, clarifying that 'ma' here is not a relative pronoun but an indefinite noun described, meaning 'water that moves a straw.' The soundest definition according to most scholars is 'what people consider flowing.' The ruling: one may perform wudu from flowing water if no trace of impurity is seen; the trace is defined as taste, color, or odor. The proof is rational: the trace indicates the presence of impurity. The passage also discusses that flowing water does not become impure by mere falling of impurity, only if a trace appears. Al-Zayla'i notes that the pronoun in 'yutawadda' minhu' could also refer to still water of ten by ten cubits, which is a related issue. The commentary then addresses a grammatical objection about the word 'ma' and concludes it is invalid. The overall rule: if a trace appears, the water is impure; if not, it is pure, regardless of whether the impurity is visible or invisible, as long as the trace is not evident.

Key Terms
التبنة (al-tibna): A single straw; used to define flowing water as water that moves a straw by its flow.
الأثر (al-athar): The trace of impurity: taste, color, or odor that appears in water after impurity falls.
الماء الجاري (al-ma' al-jari): Flowing water, defined as water that moves a straw by its flow or what people commonly consider flowing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) When the volume of water is less than ten by ten cubits.

Key Takeaway
Flowing water is defined by its ability to move a straw, and it remains pure for wudu unless a trace of impurity (taste, color, or odor) becomes perceptible.
