Flowing Water and the Ruling on Impurity Trace
Definition and ruling on flowing water purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of flowing water and apply the ruling on impurity trace to determine when wudu is valid.

Core Idea
Flowing water remains pure for wudu until a trace of impurity appears.

This lesson covers the opening of the Book of Purity in Kanz al-Daqa'iq. We learn how Hanafi scholars define flowing water and the critical principle that mere contact with impurity does not render water impure; only a change in its properties does. Understanding this prevents unnecessary hardship and ensures correct practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of flowing water
The passage defines flowing water as water that carries away a straw when thrown. The author clarifies that the most correct definition is simply 'what people consider flowing.'
The grammatical clarification about 'maa' being a nakira mawsufa resolves a false objection.
2
Understand the ruling on impurity
When an impurity falls into flowing water, wudu remains valid unless a trace (taste, color, or smell) of the impurity is perceived. The word 'seen' is interpreted as 'known' because sight cannot detect taste or smell.
This applies to both visible impurities (e.g., dead animal) and non-visible ones.
3
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a bird falls into a flowing stream and the water shows no change in taste, color, or smell, wudu is permissible. If any of these properties change, the water is impure.
Standing water of 10x10 cubits follows a similar rule per Al-Zayla'i.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة
وهو ما يذهب بتبنة ويتوضأ منه إن لم ير أثره وهو طعم أو لون أو ريح وموت ما لا دم
قوله: (وهو ما يذهب بتبنة) أي الماء الجاري ما يذهب بتبنة، وقد توهم بعض المشتغلين أن هذا الحد فاسد لأنه يرد عليه الجمل والسفينة فإنهما يذهبان بتين كثير، ومنشأ التوهم أن «ما» موصولة في كلامه وقد وقع مثلها في عبارة ابن الحاجب فإنه قال : الكلام ما يتضمن كلمتين بالاسناد. فقيل يرد عليه الورقة والحجر المكتوب عليه كلمتان فأكثر لأن «ما» موصولة بمعنى الذي لكن الجواب عنهما أن «ما» ليست موصولة وإنما هي نكرة موصوفة، فالمعنى الجاري ماء بالمد يذهب بتبنة والكلام لفظ يتضمن كلمتين. وقد اختلف في حد الجاري على أقوال منها ما ذكره المصنف وأصحها أنه ما يعده الناس جارياً كما ذكره في البدائع والتبيين وكثير من الكتب قوله: (فيتوضأ منه) أي من الماء الجاري. قال الزيلعي : ويجوز أن يعود إلى الماء الراكد الذي بلغ عشراً في عشر لأنه يجوز الوضوء به في موضع الوقوع ما لم يتغير في رواية وهو المختار عندهم. قوله : (إن لم ير أثره) أي إن لم يعلم أثر النجس فيه . ورأى تستعمل بمعنى علم قال الشاعر :
رأيت الله أكبر كل شيء
وإنما قلنا هذا لأن الطعم والرائحة لا تعلق للبصر بهما وإنما الطعم للذوق والرائحة للشم . قوله : (وهو طعم أو لون أو ريح) أي الأثر ما ذكر وحاصله أن الماء الجاري وما هو في حكمه إذا وقعت فيه نجاسة . إن ظهر أثرها لا يجوز الوضوء به وإلا جاز لأن وجود الأثر دليل وجود النجاسة، فكل ما تيقنا فيه نجاسة أو غلب على ظننا ذلك لا يجوز الوضوء به، جارياً كان أو غيره، لأن الماء الجاري لا يتنجس بوقوع النجاسة فيه كما قد يتوهم. وظاهر ما في المتون أن الجاري إذا وقعت فيه نجاسة يجوز الوضوء به إن لم ير أثرها، سواء كان النجس جيفة مرئية أو غيرها.
Book of Purity. And it is that which carries away a straw, and wudu is performed from it if its trace is not seen, and it is taste, color, or smell, and the death of that which has no blood. His statement: (And it is that which carries away a straw) meaning the flowing water that carries away a straw. Some students have imagined that this definition is flawed because the camel and the ship are objections, as they carry away many straws. The source of the imagination is that 'maa' is a relative pronoun in his speech, and a similar case occurred in the expression of Ibn al-Hajib, for he said: Speech is that which contains two words with attribution. So it was said that the objection is the leaf and the stone on which two or more words are written, because 'maa' is a relative pronoun meaning 'that which'. But the answer to both is that 'maa' is not a relative pronoun; rather, it is an indefinite noun described. So the meaning is: The flowing is water by extension that carries away a straw, and speech is a phrase that includes two words. There has been disagreement about the definition of flowing according to sayings, among them what the author mentioned, and the most correct is that it is what people consider flowing, as mentioned in al-Bada'i' and al-Tabyin and many books. His statement: (then wudu is performed from it) meaning from the flowing water. Al-Zayla'i said: It is permissible for the pronoun to refer back to the standing water that reaches ten by ten, because wudu is permissible with it at the place of occurrence as long as it does not change, in one transmission, and it is the chosen view with them. His statement: (if its trace is not seen) meaning if the trace of impurity in it is not known. 'Seen' is used in the meaning of 'known', as the poet said: 'I saw that God is greater than everything.' We only say this because taste and smell are not related to sight; rather, taste is for the tongue and smell for the nose. His statement: (and it is taste, color, or smell) meaning the trace is what is mentioned. The summary is that flowing water and that which is in its ruling, when an impurity falls into it, if its trace appears, wudu is not permissible with it; otherwise, it is permissible, because the existence of the trace is evidence of the existence of impurity. So everything in which we are certain of impurity or it is our strong assumption, wudu is not permissible with it, whether it is flowing or otherwise, because flowing water does not become impure by the fall of impurity into it as one might imagine. The apparent meaning of the texts is that when impurity falls into flowing water, wudu is permissible with it if its trace is not seen, whether the impurity is a visible dead animal or other than it.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Purity with the definition of flowing water (al-ma' al-jari) and the ruling on its use when an impurity falls into it. The author, Ibn Nujaym, is commenting on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq. He first clarifies the definition: flowing water is that which carries away a straw when thrown into it, indicating current. A common misunderstanding is that the word 'maa' in the definition is a relative pronoun, which would make the definition vulnerable to objections like a ship. The author explains that 'maa' is actually an indefinite noun described, so the meaning is 'a water that carries away a straw.' He then notes that the most correct definition is simply 'what people consider flowing,' as in al-Bada'i'. Next, he discusses the pronoun in 'fayatawadda minhu' (then wudu is performed from it). Al-Zayla'i allows it to refer to standing water of 10x10 cubits, as that is the threshold for not being polluted. The ruling is that if a trace of impurity (taste, color, or smell) is not perceived, the water is considered pure and wudu is valid. The word 'yara' (seen) is interpreted as 'y'lam' (known), because sight cannot perceive taste or smell. The key principle: the mere fall of impurity into flowing water does not render it impure; only a change in its properties does. This applies whether the impurity is a dead animal or something else.

Key Terms
ماء جاري (ma' jari): Flowing water that, by its current, carries away an object like a straw. The relied-upon definition is 'what people consider flowing.'
أثر (athar): The trace of impurity, specifically a change in taste, color, or smell. Only these three properties are considered; mere mixing does not render water impure.
نكرة موصوفة (nakira mawsufa): An indefinite noun described by a following adjective or relative clause, e.g., 'water which carries away a straw.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does flowing water become impure due to a fallen impurity?
A) Immediately when the impurity falls, even without any visible change.
B) Only when the taste, color, or smell of the water changes.
C) Only when the water turns color.
D) Only when the impurity is a dead animal.

Key Takeaway
Flowing water remains pure for wudu unless a trace of impurity appears in its taste, color, or smell.

Dead Animal in Flowing Water: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Size vs. Trace
Presents Hanafi internal dispute on ruling when a dead animal lies in flowing water

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi positions on a dead animal in flowing water and identify the tarjih mechanism between them.

Core Idea
For dead animals in flowing water, the Hanafi school divides: does size matter or only traces?

You know the general rule: flowing water is pure unless a trace of impurity appears. But what if a dead dog blocks a stream without changing the water's taste, color, or smell? This passage reveals a significant intra-Hanafi dispute: some imams say the water remains pure because the trace is absent, while others say it becomes impure because the dead animal occupies most of the flow. The dispute hinges on how to apply the hadith 'Water is pure, nothing makes it impure' and whether size itself can override the absence of a visible trace. Let's walk through the positions and the later tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
Start with the base ruling from earlier texts: flowing water is pure unless a trace of impurity appears in taste, color, or smell. This statement appears at the beginning of the passage.
This is the default for most impurities, but dead animals become the exception.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's Position
Abu Yusuf holds that only the trace matters: if a dead dog blocks a small stream but the water below shows no change, wudu is permissible. His evidence is the general hadith applied to flowing water without needing a specific indicator (mukhtass).
His view is reported in al-Yanabi' and later favored by Fath al-Qadir.
3
Understand the Majority Position (Abu Hanifa & Muhammad)
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad look at the proportion of water flowing over the dead animal. If all or most of the water flows over it, wudu is not permissible; if a small part, it is permissible. For half, qiyas says permissible but istihsan says not (the cautious view).
This is recorded in many later fatwa collections like al-Tanjis and al-Bada'i'.
4
Analyze the Half Case and Istihsan
When the dead animal occupies half the flow, there is a conflict: analogy (qiyas) would permit wudu because the impurity does not predominate, but juristic preference (istihsan) prohibits it because it is safer. The later school leans toward istihsan as more cautious.
This shows how istihsan can override qiyas in Hanafi methodology.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih (Preponderance)
Fath al-Qadir and Allamah Qasim side with Abu Yusuf, arguing that the hadith's generality needs no special restriction. However, the majority of later fatwa books authenticate the size-based view. Both positions exist, but the student should know the dominant view in the school.
The teacher notes that the most correct (al-asahh) per al-Tanjis is the size-based view, but Fath al-Qadir's view is also respected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا بال إنسان فيه فتوضأ آخر من أسفله جاز ما لم يظهر في الجرية أثره. قال محمد في كتاب الأشربة : ولو كسرت خابية خمر في الفرات ورجل يتوضأ أسفل منه فما لم يجد في الماء طعم الخمر أو ريحه أو لونه يجوز الوضوء به، وكذا لو استقرت المرئية فيه بأن كانت جيفة إن ظهر أثر النجاسة لا يجوز وإلا جاز، سواء أخذت الجيفة الجرية أو نصفها إنما العبرة لظهور الأثر. ويوافقه ما في الينابيع. قال أبو يوسف في ساقية صغيرة فيها كلب ميت سد عرضها فيجري الماء فوقه وتحته إنه لا بأس بالوضوء أسفل منه إذا لم يتغير طعمه أو لونه أو ريحه . وقيل : ينبغي أن يكون هذا قول أبي يوسف خاصة. أما عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد لا يجوز الوضوء أسفل من الكلب ا هـ ما في الينابيع. لكن المذكور في الفتاوى كفتاوى قاضيخان والتنجيس والولوالجي والخلاصة. وفي البدائع : وكثير من كتب أئمتنا الأثر إنما يعتبر في غير الجيفة، أما في الجيفة فإنه ينظر ، إن كان كله أو أكثره يجري عليها لا يجوز الوضوء به، وإن كان الأقل يجوز الوضوء، وإن كان النصف فالقياس الجواز والاستحسان أنه لا يجوز وهو الأحوط . ونظر هذا ماء المطر إذا جرى في ميزاب من السطح وكان على السطح عذرة فالماء طاهر لأن الذي يجري على غير العذرة أكثر، وإن كانت العذرة عند الميزاب فإن كان الماء كله أو أكثره أو نصفه يلاقي العذرة فهو نجس، وإن كان أكثره لا يلاقي العذرة فهو طاهر، وكذا أيضاً ماء المطر إذا جرى على عذرات واستنقع في موضع كان الجواب كذلك. ورجح في فتح القدير أن العبرة لظهور الأثر مطلقاً لأن الحديث وهو قوله الماء طهور لا ينجسه شيء» لما حمل على الجاري كان مقتضاه جواز التوضؤ من أسفله وإن أخذت الجيفة أكثر الماء ولم يتغير فقولهم إذا أخذت الجيفة أكثر الماء أو نصفه لا يجوز يحتاج إلى مخصص قال : يوافقه ما عن أبي يوسف وقد نقلناه عن الينابيع . وقال تلميذه العلامة قاسم في رسالته المختار اعتبار ما عن أبي يوسف ا هـ . لكن لقائل أن يقول : الأوجه ما في أكثر الكتب وقد صححه في التنجيس لصاحب الهداية لأن العلماء الله عنهم إنما قالوا بأن الماء الجاري إذا وقعت فيه نجاسة يجوز الوضوء به إذا لم ير أثرها رضي
If a person urinates in it, and another performs wudu below it, it is permissible as long as its trace does not appear in the flow. Muhammad said in the Book of Drinks: If a clay vessel of wine is broken in the Euphrates, and a man performs wudu below it, as long as he does not find in the water the taste, smell, or color of the wine, wudu is permissible. Similarly, if a visible object settles in it, such as a dead animal, if the trace of impurity appears, it is not permissible; otherwise it is permissible, regardless of whether the dead animal takes up the current or half of it; consideration is only given to the appearance of the trace. What is in al-Yanabi' agrees with this. Abu Yusuf said regarding a small stream in which a dead dog blocks its width, and water flows over and under it: there is no harm in performing wudu below it if its taste, color, or smell has not changed. It is said that this should be the statement of Abu Yusuf specifically. As for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, wudu is not permissible below the dog. End of what is in al-Yanabi'. But what is mentioned in the fatwa collections such as the Fatawa of Qadikhan, al-Tanjis, al-Walwaliji, and al-Khulasa, and in al-Bada'i' and many books of our imams, the trace is only considered in other than a dead animal. As for a dead animal, consideration is given: if all or most of the water flows over it, wudu is not permissible; if less, it is permissible; if half, by qiyas it is permissible, by istihsan it is not permissible, and that is more cautious. An analog of this is rainwater flowing through a roof spout when there is excrement on the roof: the water is pure because most of what flows is not over the excrement. If the excrement is at the spout, then if all or most or half of the water meets the excrement, it is impure; if most does not meet it, it is pure. Similarly, rainwater that flows over excrement and collects in a place has the same ruling. In Fath al-Qadir, it was preponderated that consideration is given to the appearance of the trace absolutely, because the hadith, 'Water is pure, nothing makes it impure,' when applied to flowing water, its implication is the permissibility of performing wudu from below it even if the dead animal takes up most of the water and it does not change. Their statement that if the dead animal takes up most of the water or half, it is not permissible, needs a specific indicator. He said: What is from Abu Yusuf agrees with this, and we have transmitted it from al-Yanabi'. His student, the scholar Qasim, said in his treatise: The chosen view is to consider what is from Abu Yusuf. End. But one may say: The most correct is what is in most books, and al-Tanjis of the author of al-Hidayah has authenticated it, because the scholars, may Allah be pleased with them, said that if impurity falls into flowing water, wudu is permissible if its trace is not seen.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a dead animal (jifa) in flowing water. The starting point is the general rule: wudu is permissible if no trace of impurity appears. Muhammad al-Shaybani, in his Book of Drinks (Kitab al-Ashriba), applies this rule to a case of spilled wine in the Euphrates: only the appearance of taste, color, or smell prevents wudu. He explicitly states that the size of the dead animal—whether it takes up the entire current or half—is irrelevant; only the trace matters. Abu Yusuf agrees, citing a case of a dead dog blocking a small stream: wudu below is fine if no change in the water's properties. However, al-Yanabi' reports that this is specifically Abu Yusuf's view; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prohibit wudu below the dog. The later fatwa collections (Fatawa Qadikhan, al-Tanjis, etc.) and al-Bada'i' record a different position: for a dead animal, unlike other impurities, one looks at how much water flows over it. If all or most, no wudu; if less, permissible; if half, there is a conflict between qiyas (permissible) and istihsan (not permissible), with istihsan preferred. The same logic is applied to rainwater on a roof with excrement: if most water does not contact the impurity, it is pure. The dispute is resolved by Fath al-Qadir, which reasserts the absolute standard of trace appearance, citing the hadith 'Water is pure, nothing makes it impure' (applied to flowing water). This agrees with Abu Yusuf. The author's student, Allamah Qasim, also adopts this view in his treatise. However, a counter-argument is that the majority of books and the authentication in al-Tanjis support the majority view that size matters. Thus, the school has tension between the early (Abu Yusuf) and later (majority) positions.

Key Terms
جيفة (jifa): A dead animal that has no flowing blood; specifically a carcass that is considered najis al-ayn (impure in itself) in the Hanafi school.
الجرية (al-jariya): The current or flow of water; the stream being considered.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; here it is used to prefer the ruling that prohibits wudu when half the water flows over the jifa, overriding qiyas.
مخصص (mukhtass): A specific indicator that would narrow the general meaning of a text. In the dispute, Abu Yusuf supporters argue that the general ruling of the hadith ('water is pure') needs no mukhass to exclude the size case; the opposition claims that their ruling is based on an understood mukhass.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of later Hanafi books (as reported in the text), if a dead animal occupies half of a flowing stream, what is the ruling on wudu below it?
A) Wudu is absolutely permissible because no trace appears.
B) By qiyas it is permissible, but by istihsan it is not, and the cautious view is to avoid.
C) Wudu is not permissible because half is like all.
D) Wudu is permissible only if the water flows over the animal.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute: for a dead animal in flowing water, the majority consider size (all/most = impure), while Abu Yusuf and some later authorities consider only the appearance of a trace.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
The Carcass in Running Water: Trace or Certainty? – An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on trace vs certainty for carcasses in running water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and the Sahibayn on using running water downstream of a visible carcass, and identify the mufta bihi according to the hierarchy of Hanafi sources.

Core Idea
For running water, impurity trace is the criterion – not mere certainty – but the Sahibayn hold otherwise.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: does running water become impure if a dead animal lies in it but no taste, color, or smell changes downstream? Abu Yusuf says no; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes. The author navigates through primary texts (mutun), commentaries, and fatwa compilations to resolve the tension, ultimately preferring the view that appearance of trace (al-taghayyur) is the decisive factor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed ruling
What is the specific act being ruled upon? It is performing wudu downstream from a visible impurity (carcass) in running water. The dispute: is the water permissible if no trace appears?
The impurity is a carcass (jayfah), not a liquid like wine.
2
State Abu Yusuf’s position and evidence
Abu Yusuf holds that wudu is permissible as long as no taste, color, or smell change is detected. His evidence: the hadith 'Water is pure, nothing makes it impure' applied to flowing water, and qiyas that the impurity is carried away.
This is reported in al-Yanabi' and supported by Imam Muhammad’s example of wine in the Euphrates.
3
State the Sahibayn’s position and evidence
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that wudu is not permissible downstream of a visible carcass, even without trace. Their evidence: certainty of the presence of impurity, which overrides the general hadith because the hadith is conditional on absence of change.
Their view is based on istihsan and the principle that certainty of impurity requires caution.
4
Analyze the later fatwa distinction
Later fatwa works (e.g., Fatawa Qadikhan) distinguish between carcass and non-carcass. For carcasses, they look at the proportion of water flowing over it: if all or most flows over, water is impure; if less, pure; if half, qiyas permits but istihsan forbids (the latter being preferred).
This is a synthesis trying to reconcile the two positions.
5
Evaluate the tarjih by the author
The author (of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) favors the view that trace is the absolute criterion, citing Ibn al-Humam (Fath al-Qadir) and the mutun. He argues that the hadith, when applied to flowing water, permits use unless trace appears. He acknowledges the fatwa precaution but insists that the primary texts take precedence.
The hierarchy: mutun > commentaries > fatwa collections.

Etc. If a person urinates in it and another performs wudu from below it, it is permissible as long as no trace appears in the stream. Muhammad said in Kitab al-Ashriba: If a jar of wine is broken in the Euphrates and a man performs wudu downstream, as long as he does not find taste, smell, or color of wine in the water, wudu with it is permissible. Similarly, if a visible impurity like a carcass settles in it, if the trace of impurity appears it is not permissible, otherwise it is permissible, whether the carcass takes the stream or half of it; only the appearance of trace matters. And what is in al-Yanabi' agrees with this. Abu Yusuf said about a small canal in which there is a dead dog blocking its width, water flows over and under it: there is no harm in performing wudu downstream if its taste, color, or smell has not changed. It is said: This should be specifically the view of Abu Yusuf. As for Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, wudu downstream of the dog is not permissible. End quote from al-Yanabi'. But what is mentioned in the fatwa collections like Fatawa Qadikhan, al-Tanjis, al-Walwaliji, and al-Khulasa, and in al-Bada'i' and many books of our imams: that trace is only considered for non-carcass impurities. As for a carcass, consideration is: if all or most of it flows over the carcass, wudu with it is not permissible; if less, it is permissible; if half, analogically it is permissible, but by istihsan it is not permissible, which is more precautionary. The analogy of this is rainwater flowing through a gutter from a roof on which there is dung: the water is pure because what flows over non-dung is more. If the dung is at the gutter opening, then if all, most, or half of the water meets the dung, it is impure; if most does not meet it, it is pure. Similarly for rainwater flowing over dung and pooling in a place. In Fath al-Qadir, it is preferred that the criterion is the appearance of trace absolutely, because the hadith 'Water is pure, nothing makes it impure' when interpreted as flowing water necessitates permission of wudu from its lower part even if the carcass takes most of the water and it has not changed. Their saying that if the carcass takes most or half of the water it is not permissible requires a specific proof. He said: agreeing with this is what is from Abu Yusuf, which we have transmitted from al-Yanabi'. And his student, the scholar Qasim, said in his treatise: the chosen view is considering Abu Yusuf's view. End quote. But one may say: the most appropriate is what is in most books, and it was corrected in al-Tanjis by the author of al-Hidayah, because the scholars said that when an impurity falls into running water, wudu with it is permissible if its trace is not seen... Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a critical commentary on the rulings regarding running water contaminated by impurities, especially carcasses. It begins by stating the general principle: if impurity falls into running water and no trace (taste, smell, color) appears downstream, using the water is permissible. This is supported by quotes from Imam Muhammad (regarding wine in a river) and Abu Yusuf (regarding a dead dog in a small canal). However, it is noted that this unconditional allowance is specifically Abu Yusuf's view; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not permit use downstream when a visible impurity like a dead dog is present, even if no trace appears. The passage then presents the fatwa literature position: for non-carcass impurities, the criterion is trace; for carcasses, if all or most of the water flows over the carcass, it is impure; if less, pure; if half, there is difference between qiyas (permissible) and istihsan (impermissible). The author then reports that Fath al-Qadir (by Ibn al-Humam) prefers the absolute reliance on trace, arguing that the hadith 'Water is pure, nothing makes it impure'—interpreted for flowing water—implies permissibility unless trace appears. The author of al-Nahr objects, stating that for carcasses, certainty of impurity presence should override the lack of trace. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq rebuts this, arguing that for running water, mere certainty of impurity is insufficient unless there is prevailing assumption that the water used actually flowed over the impurity. He then cites al-Nabulsi who distinguishes between invisible impurities (where trace is necessary) and visible ones like carcasses (where presence itself may necessitate impurity). Ultimately, the author suggests that the apparent meaning of the primary texts (mutun) supports the criterion of trace absolutely, and that these primary texts take precedence over commentaries and fatwa collections. A cautionary note is added regarding snow water flowing over impurity. Throughout, the passage demonstrates the layered methodology of Hanafi legal reasoning: reliance on hadith, qiyas, istihsan, and hierarchical authority of sources.

Key Terms
جيفة (jayfah): A carcass of a dead animal that is visible in water
جاري (jārī): Running water that flows continuously
التغير (al-taghayyur): Change in one of three qualities: taste, color, or smell of water due to impurity

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa collections cited in the passage, if exactly half of the water in a stream flows over a carcass, what is the ruling by istihsan?
A) Water is permissible
B) Water is not permissible
C) Water is permissible only if no trace appears
D) It depends on the size of the carcass

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the ruling on running water contaminated by a visible carcass is disputed: Abu Yusuf relies solely on trace appearance, while the Sahibayn and many fatwa collections consider the proportion of water flowing over the impurity, with istihsan often preferring caution.

Running Water and Corpse Impurity: When Certainty Overrides Change
Explanation of running water corpse exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on running water that flows over a corpse and explain why apparent change is not required when certainty of direct contact exists.

Core Idea
Running water is not automatically impure by contact; only when impurity overcomes it or certainty of direct contact exists—as with a corpse.

In this passage, the author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on a key exception in Hanafi taharah: running water that flows over a corpse becomes impure even if no change in color, taste, or odor appears. This seems to contradict the general principle that water remains pure until it changes, but the exception is justified by the certainty (yaqeen) of impurity when water flows over most or half of a corpse. You will examine the proof from consensus and the internal Hanafi debate about the role of change.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for running water and impurity
Recall that in the Hanafi school, running water does not become impure just by contact with an impurity; the impurity must overcome the water, manifesting a change in its properties. This is the default principle.
This rule is based on the hadith 'Water is pure until it changes.'
2
Identify the exception for a corpse and its reasoning
When a corpse (jifa) is present and water flows over most or half of it, we have certainty that the water directly contacted the impurity. The text argues that change is merely a sign of impurity; when certainty exists, the sign is unnecessary.
The exception is supported by consensus (ijma) that the hadith applies only when change is possible.
3
Trace the proof: consensus (ijma) applied to the hadith
The hadith 'Water is pure until it changes' is interpreted by consensus that change is the indicator of impurity. But for a corpse, certainty of impurity overrides the need for change. Thus, consensus 'specifies' (yukhasṣiṣ) the general rule.
The consensus is considered a proof (dalil) even though its transmission is zanni.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute about mere certainty vs. change
Some Hanafi authorities (e.g., al-Nahr, al-Fath) argue that running water is never impure without apparent effect, regardless of certainty. The author rebuts by distinguishing between theoretical certainty and predominant assumption (ghalabat al-zann) based on the amount of flow.
Al-Nabulsi further differentiates visible impurities (like a corpse) from invisible ones (like urine), where change is required.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and the tarjih
The text concludes that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is to consider the apparent effect absolutely, as given in the matn texts, preferred by Ibn al-Humam and others. Fatawa follow this view.
This illustrates the hierarchy: matn > commentaries > fatawa.

Book of Purification. Because impurity does not settle with the flow of water, so when its effect does not appear, it is known that the water removed its essence and its essence did not remain, so using the water is permissible. But if the impurity is a corpse and water flows over most of it or half of it, we are certain of the existence of impurity in it, and it has preceded that everything we are certain of the existence of impurity in or our predominant assumption of its existence in is not permissible to use. So this is taken from the indication of consensus, because the hadith was carried by consensus upon water that did not change, because at the time of change we are certain of the existence of impurity, so change is a proof of the existence of impurity in what it is possible. Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a complex expansion on the ruling of running water that comes into contact with impurity, particularly a corpse (jifa). The commentator begins by stating the well-known Hanafi principle: impurity does not settle in flowing water; therefore, as long as no trace of the impurity appears (in color, taste, or odor), the water remains pure and permissible for use. However, when the impurity is a sizable corpse and water flows over most or half of it, the certainty (yaqeen) of the water having contacted the impurity arises. Citing consensus (ijma), the author argues that in such cases the water becomes impure regardless of whether any change in its properties is observed, because change is merely a sign (alama) of impurity; its absence does not prove the absence of impurity. The commentator then engages with an objection from al-Nahr: if flowing water is not affected by impurity unless it overcomes the water by manifesting its effect, then why treat flowing over most/half differently from flowing over less? The rebuttal distinguishes between theoretical certainty and practical predominant assumption (ghalabat al-zann): when water flows over most of a corpse, it is predominantly assumed that the water has directly contacted the corpse, whereas when it flows over a lesser part, this assumption is weaker. The discussion then cites Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulsi, who differentiates between visible and invisible impurities: for invisible impurities like urine, change is a necessary condition; for visible ones like a corpse, change is not required because the presence of the impurity itself is manifest. Finally, the commentator prioritises the matn texts (which consider the appearance of effect absolutely) over commentaries and fatwas, noting that the muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam and others upheld this view, and it is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
الجيفة (al-jifa): A corpse or carcass, considered a visible impurity in the water.
الماء الجاري (al-ma' al-jari): Running water that is in motion, not stagnant; the ruling on it differs from still water.
التغير (al-taghyyur): Change in one of the three properties of water: color, taste, or odor, indicating the presence of impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The water is impure only if the corpse is visible in the water.
D) The water is pure because running water never becomes impure from contact with a corpse.

Key Takeaway
If running water passes over most of a corpse, the water is impure even without apparent change, due to certainty of contact.

Fatwa on Water Mixed with Dung in Channels
Fatwa on water mixed with dung in channels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criteria (change in properties, flow, and cistern size) to determine the purity of water mixed with dung in distribution channels.

Core Idea
Dung in flowing water: pure if no effect appears; in stagnant cisterns: impure unless continuous flow or transformation into humus.

This passage presents a detailed fatwa addressing a common problem in Damascus: water in channels (qasatil) mixed with animal dung (sarqin). The mufti synthesizes principles from earlier sources to provide practical guidance. The ruling hinges on whether the water shows any sign of impurity (color, taste, odor), the size of the container, and whether the water is flowing or stagnant. Understanding these nuanced conditions is essential for dealing with real-world water contamination while avoiding undue hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the impurity and water source
Recognize that the impurity is dung (sarqin) and the water is distributed through channels (qasatil) from a common division point (tali').
Dung is a liquid impurity in the Hanafi school, but its effect can be diluted or transformed.
2
Distinguish flowing vs. stagnant water
Determine whether the water is continuously flowing (like channels) or becomes stagnant in a cistern.
Flow affects whether impurity can spread or be removed.
3
Assess change in water properties
Check if the water's color, taste, or odor has changed due to the dung. If unchanged, the water may be pure; if changed, it is impure.
This is the primary criterion from earlier sources.
4
Consider cistern size for stagnant water
For water that has settled in a cistern, apply the size rule: if the cistern is less than the 'abundant quantity' (qillatayn, about 20 cubic cubits), any settled dung makes the entire body impure even if the water appears clear.
Small cisterns cannot self-purify.
5
Evaluate continuous flow and transformation
If clear water continuously enters and exits the cistern, the water gradually becomes pure. Also, if the dung turns into humus (ham'a, black mud), it no longer actively pollutes.
These are leniencies based on the principle of hardship bringing ease.

Caution: Here is an important issue, there is no harm in presenting it, even if its mention is lengthy, it is excused due to the severe need for it. We say: The scholar Abd al-Rahman Afandi al-Imadi, Mufti of Damascus, said in his book Hadiyat Ibn al-Imad: a question. The author of Majma' al-Fatawa in al-Khazanah said: Snow water, if it flows on a path with dung and impurity, if the impurity disappears and mixes until its effect is not seen, then one may perform ablution with it, even if it was impure. If the water is abundant and one does not see what is beneath it, it is pure; if it is seen, it is impure. And in al-Multaqat it says: its minimum is that ablution is permissible if the effect of the impurity does not appear, and their saying 'if the corpse takes most or half, it is not permissible' means even if the effect of the impurity does not appear. As for performing ablution at a spring from which water emerges, if at the point of emergence it is permissible, and if elsewhere, likewise if its quantity is four by four or less. And if the water... some mashayikh: water is pure even if little as long as it is flowing. I say: these issues are used for reference because the affliction is widespread in our land due to their habit of mixing water with dung of animals, so memorize them, for they are the closest we have found in that after thorough search in reliable books, and that this is among the most important matters, especially when added to it what Ibn Nujaym and others mentioned regarding the branches of the famous principle, namely their saying 'hardship brings ease', including excusing the impurity of one who is excused and not judging water to be impure if it contacts a mutanajjis except after separation, and what they mentioned regarding the ruling of purity in istinja, even though every time water contacts impurity it becomes impure, and that water is not harmed by change due to staying, mud, algae, and everything difficult to guard against. He said: and my master al-Arif has elaborated in his commentary, but I will mention what is needed for explaining this place. We say: al-sarqin is dung, and the meaning of 'the impurity disappears' is that its effect does not appear. This is based on not stipulating continuous flow in running water. The apparent meaning of his saying 'does not see what is beneath it' is that one does not see the impurity in the bottom of the river; if it is seen and water passes over it, it is like a corpse, and its requirement is the impurity of that water even if it is flowing. What he quoted from al-Multaqat means al-Multaqat meaning: when the effect of the impurity does not appear, and this is like the other opinion in the issue of the corpse, looking at the appearance of the effect or not. The outcome of the discussion, based on what is widespread in trials, is that consideration is given to the change of one of the properties by the impurity of dung or its absence. So if the dung is placed in the water distribution point to houses, called al-tali', and flows with the water in the channels (qasatil), then the water is impure. If the dung settles in the middle of the channels and the water flows clear, it is analogous to the case of snow water flowing over impurity or the riverbed being impure and water flows over it without any of its properties changing: that water is all pure, and similarly this. When the water reaches the cisterns in the houses: if it arrives with one of its properties changed by dung or the essence of dung is apparent in it, it is impure without doubt. If it settles in a cistern smaller than the abundant quantity (qillatayn), it is impure, even if it clears afterwards and its change disappears by itself, because it is impure water and impure water does not become pure by the disappearance of its change by itself, especially since dung has settled in its bottom. If it settles in a large cistern, it is also impure as long as it is changed or its change disappears by itself. But if water continues to flow afterwards until the water comes clear and the change of the cistern is removed by that clear water, then all the water becomes pure, whether the cistern is small or large. If the dung is settled in its bottom while the clear water in that cistern enters from one place and exits from another, then if the flow stops afterwards and the cistern is small with dung settled in its bottom, the cistern remains impure until the dung in its bottom turns into humus (ham'a), which is black mud; then it is not impure at that point. If the cistern is large, then the matter is as we practice with ourselves in this issue since we have been afflicted with it and found no explicit transmission. End of his speech, may his secret be sanctified. I say: and the meaning of his saying 'the cistern remains impure until the dung in its bottom turns into humus, so it is not impure then' means it becomes pure if it flows afterwards, not merely by turning into humus, as is known from... Then he said, in a little... pass.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a practical fatwa addressing a widespread problem in Damascus: water in distribution channels (qasatil) mixed with animal dung (sarqin). The mufti Abd al-Rahman al-Imadi, citing earlier sources like Majma' al-Fatawa and al-Multaqat, rules that the key determinant is whether the impure effect (change in color, taste, or odor) is apparent. If dung is placed at the water division point and flows visibly mixed, the water is impure. If the dung settles and clear water flows over it, the water is pure, analogous to snow water flowing over a contaminated riverbed. When water reaches house cisterns: if it arrives with any change or visible dung, it is impure; if it settles in a small cistern (less than the amount that prevents impurity, about 20 cubic cubits), it remains impure even if it clears, because the settled dung continues to pollute. In a large cistern, the same applies. However, if continuous flow of clear water enters and exits the cistern, it becomes pure over time. If the dung turns into humus (black mud) and the water is flowing, the water becomes pure. These detailed rulings reflect the Hanafi principle of hardship bringing ease (mashaqqa tujlib al-taysir), allowing leniency in situations where it is difficult to completely avoid impurity.

Key Terms
السرقين (al-sarqin): Dung, specifically animal manure, considered an impurity in this context.
القساطل (al-qasatil): Water channels or pipes used to distribute water from a main source to houses.
الحمأة (al-ham'a): Black mud that forms from decomposed dung; it is not considered impure in terms of rendering water impure if the water flows.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa, what happens to a small cistern (less than the abundant quantity) that has clear water but settled dung at the bottom, and then the dung turns into humus (black mud) without any flow?
A) The water becomes pure immediately.
B) The water remains impure until the dung is physically removed.
C) The water becomes pure only if the humus is also removed.
D) The water becomes pure because humus is not impure.

Key Takeaway
The purity of water mixed with dung depends on observable change, flow conditions, and cistern size; settled dung in a small cistern renders it impure until continuous flow or transformation into humus occurs.

Taharah — Purification

Water Mixed with Dung: Purity in Flowing Channels
Expands ruling on water with dung, necessity, and practical scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which water mixed with animal dung remains pure for wudu in the Hanafi school and apply the trace condition to practical scenarios.

Core Idea
In flowing water, if no trace of dung appears, the water is pure even if dung is present — necessity overrides strict impurity rules.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a practical problem: water channels contaminated with animal dung used to seal leaks. The author clarifies that the key is not the mere presence of dung, but whether it changes the water's color, taste, or smell. This distinction, rooted in the maxim 'hardship brings ease', allows for valid wudu even in common contaminated water supplies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of water and impurity
Recognize that the scenario involves flowing water (channels) and animal dung (zarqin) as the impurity. This sets the stage for applying Hanafi rules on water purity.
Flowing water is treated differently from standing water in Hanafi fiqh.
2
Apply the trace condition (taghayyur al-awṣāf)
Check whether the dung has changed the water's color, taste, or smell. If any attribute is altered, the water becomes impure (najis). If no change, the water remains pure.
This is the core criterion; presence of dung alone does not render water impure.
3
Consider the role of necessity (ḍarūrah)
When dung is used to seal channels out of necessity, the ruling is lenient: even if trace appears, some scholars pardon it because avoiding such water would cause hardship.
The maxim 'al-mashaqqa tajlib al-taysīr' applies.
4
Distinguish flowing from standing water in basins
Water that settles in basins requires different analysis: if clear water continues flowing in and out, it eventually purifies the basin. If flow stops, the basin may remain impure until the dung transforms into harmless mud.
The author gives detailed scenarios for small and large basins.
5
Derive the muftā bihī ruling
The relied-upon position: water is pure if no trace of impurity appears, especially in flowing water. This is based on the dominant view that appearance of trace negates pardon, but flowing water may be excused due to necessity.
This resolves the widespread problem in the author's locality.

بانتظار صفو الماء غايته العفو عن النجاسة المستقرة في باطن القساطل إذا جرى الماء عليها صافياً على حسب ما قدمنا بيانه وعدم تنجيس الماء الطاهر بالزبل النجس للضرورة حيث لا يجري الماء إلا به لكونه يسد خروق And their statement that if the carcass takes up the majority or half, it is not permissible—they mean even if no trace of impurity appears. As for performing wudu at the source from which water flows, if it is at the place of its exit, it is permissible; if it is elsewhere, similarly if its size is four by four or less. And if the water—some mashayikh: the water is pure even if little if it is flowing. I say: These issues are used as guidance for what is widespread in our land, namely their habit of running water with animal dung (zarqin). So memorize them, for they are the closest we have obtained on this after searching and investigating in reliable books, and that this is among the most important matters, especially when combined with what Ibn Nujaym and others mentioned in the branches of the famous maxim, namely their saying 'Hardship brings ease': pardon of the impurity of the excused one, and not deeming water impure when it contacts a mutanajjis except upon separation, and what they mentioned regarding the ruling of purity in istinja' despite that every time water contacts impurity it becomes impure, and that water is not harmed by change due to staying, mud, algae, and everything that is difficult to protect from. End. And here my master the gnostic has been lengthy in his commentary, but I will mention from it what is needed for explaining this place. So we say: Zarqin is dung, and the meaning of the impurity being 'ghayyabat' is that no trace of it appears. And this is based on not requiring a large amount in flowing water. And it appears that the meaning of his statement 'what is beneath it is not seen' is that the impurity which is in the bed of the river is not seen, so that even if it is seen and water passes over it, it is likened to a carcass, and the consequence is that water is impure even if flowing. And what he reported from al-Multaqat means: al-Multaqat minhu if no trace of impurity appears. And this is like the other opinion in the issue of the carcass that looks to the appearance or absence of trace. And the summary of the discourse on what is widespread is that change in any one of the three attributes due to the impurity of zarqin and its absence is considered. If the zarqin is placed at the water divider to houses and the like, called the 'tali', and it flows with the water in the channels, then the water is impure. If the dung settles in the middle of the channels and the water flows clear, it is analogous to the case of water from snow flowing over impurity or if the riverbed is impure and water flows over it without any of its attributes changing due to impurity—that water is entirely pure. And similarly this. Then when water reaches the basins in houses: if it arrives with one of the attributes changed by the dung or the substance of dung is visible in it, it is impure without doubt. If it settles in a basin less than the large quantity, it is impure. And if it becomes clear afterwards in the basin and its change disappears by itself because it is impure water—and impure water does not become pure by the removal of its change by itself, especially if the dung has settled at its bottom. And if it settles in a large basin, it is also impure as long as it is changed, or if its change disappears by itself as well. As for when water continues flowing thereafter until clear water comes and the basin's change is removed by that clear water, then all the water becomes pure, whether the basin is small or large. And if the dung is settled at its bottom as long as the clear water in that basin enters from one place and exits from another. Then if the flow ceases thereafter and the basin is small and the dung settled at its bottom, the basin remains impure until the dung at its bottom becomes ham'a (black mud), then it is no longer impure. And if the basin is large, the matter is: this is how we deal with ourselves in this issue where we are afflicted by it and we have not found explicit transmission on it. End of his speech, may his secret be sanctified. I say: And the meaning of his statement 'the basin is impure until the dung at its bottom becomes ham'a, then it is no longer impure' means: if it flows after that, not merely by the dung becoming ham'a, as is known from [context]. Then he said, may his secret be sanctified: And the apparent meaning of the author's statement, may Allah have mercy on him, here is that pardon in that matter exists even if the trace of zarqin appears in water, carried on the change due to staying and the like, due to necessity. And the correct view is what we mentioned first: if the trace of impurity appears in water, then no pardon at that time due to lack of necessity to wait for the water to clear. At most, pardon is for the impurity settled in the interior of the channels when water flows over it clear according to what we have previously explained, and the non-contamination of pure water by impure dung due to necessity, since water does not flow except through it because it blocks the holes of the channels, so water does not leak from them and remains flowing over it. End. I say: And it is not hidden that on the opinion that requires appearance of trace in flowing water, it becomes pure, so there is no need to say pardon based on it.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, addressing a practical problem in the author's locality: water channels contaminated with animal dung (zarqin). The author begins by stating the general ruling: wudu is permissible if no trace of impurity appears, even if the water is flowing. He then explains that this is based on the maxim 'hardship brings ease' (المشقة تجلب التيسير). The core of the discussion revolves around whether the water's attributes (color, taste, smell) have changed due to the dung. If no change is visible, the water is considered pure, especially in flowing water where a large quantity is not required. The author provides detailed scenarios for water in channels leading to basins: if the dung settles and clear water flows over it without change, it is pure; if the water arrives with visible change, it is impure even in a large basin until the flow of clear water removes the change. For small basins with settled dung, the water remains impure until the dung transforms into ham'a (black mud). The author concludes that the correct view is to consider the appearance of trace: if visible, no pardon. However, if the water is flowing, the trace may be overlooked due to necessity, as argued by some scholars. This demonstrates the Hanafi approach of applying istihsan based on 'urf and necessity.

Key Terms
السرقين (al-zarqīn): Animal dung, specifically used to seal water channels in the context of this passage.
تغير الأوصاف (taghayyur al-awṣāf): Change in the color, taste, or smell of water due to impurity, which determines najasah.
الماء الجاري (al-mā' al-jārī): Flowing water, which in Hanafi fiqh does not require a minimum quantity (qullatayn) for purity; it is judged by appearance of trace.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Hanafi context, if water flows over a layer of animal dung in a channel but the water itself shows no change in color, taste, or smell, what is the ruling on using that water for wudu?
A) It is pure and wudu is valid.
B) It is impure because the dung touched the water.
C) It is pure only if the water is a large quantity (qullatayn).
D) It is impure because flowing water does not purify itself.

Key Takeaway
Water mixed with animal dung remains pure for wudu as long as no change in color, taste, or smell appears, particularly in flowing water where necessity may also pardon minor traces.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Impurity of Animal Dung and Necessity-Based Fatwa
Intra-Hanafi dispute on dung impurity and necessity-based fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the impurity of animal dung and explain the conditions under which Zufar's opinion may be followed for fatwa.

Core Idea
Animal dung is najis in the majority Hanafi view, but necessity permits following Zufar's opinion that all dung is pure.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical problem: water contamination with animal dung in regions where people rely on limited water sources. While the established Hanafi position holds all animal dung impure, a rare narration from Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani and the opinion of Zufar offer leniency. The author argues that fatwa may be given according to Zufar's view when necessity demands, especially for women and those with scarce water.

Guided Path
1
Identify the majority Hanafi position
Note that the default ruling from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and the apparent riwaya of Muhammad is that all animal dung (rawth and khutha) is najis (impure).
2
List the dissenting Hanafi opinions
Zufar considers dung of edible animals pure, and in another narration all dung pure. A rare narration from Muhammad also declares all dung pure due to widespread incidence (umum al-balwa).
These are legitimate ikhtilaf within the madhhab.
3
Understand the concept of 'umum al-balwa
Realize that widespread occurrence of an impurity can be a basis for leniency, making the strict ruling impractical and thus allowing a concession.
This principle is used to prefer the lenient narration.
4
Analyze the tarjih for fatwa
The author, following al-Mubtagha, argues that necessity (darura) makes it appropriate to give fatwa on Zufar's opinion (all dung pure), especially for water purity.
Similar to the fatwa on used water by Muhammad despite Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf's opposition.
5
Apply to practical scenarios
Consider cases like contaminated wells or rivers; the lenient fatwa alleviates hardship for women and those with limited water access.
The ruling extends to bathhouse basins and wells by analogy.

Source Text

ثم نقل عن ابن حجر الشافعي في شرح العباب بناء على قول الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله : إذا ضاق الأمر اتسع أنه لا يضر تغير أنهر الشام بما فيها من الزبل ولو قليلة لأنه لا يمكن جريها المضطر إليه الناس إلا به اهـ قال والظاهر من قوله «لا يضر الخ» أن المعفو عنه عنده أثر الزبل لا عينه وهذا كله بناء على نجاسة الزبل عندنا وعند الشافعي رحمه الله، ثم نقل عبارات الفقهاء في ذلك. وحاصلها أن الروث والختي عند مالك رحمه الله طاهران، وعن زفر روث ما يؤكل لحمه طاهر، وعنه أيضاً مطلقاً كمالك. ثم قال : وفي كتاب المبتغى بالغين المعجمة : الأرواث كلها نجسة إلا رواية عن محمد أنها طاهرة للبلوى وفي هذه الرواية توسعة لأرباب الدواب فقل ما يسلمون عن التلطخ بالأرواث والأخثاء فتحفظ هذه الرواية اهـ كلام المبتغى. قال: وإذا أردت تقليد من يقول بالطهارة فانظر في شروطه في باقي المسألة واعمل على ذلك. وإن قلنا بالفتوى على قول زفر في طهارة الأرواث كلها بالنسبة إلى تغير الماء في بلادنا هذه فلا يبعد لأن الضرورة داعية إلى ذلك كما أفتى علماؤنا رحمهم الله بقول محمد رحمه الله في طهارة الماء المستعمل لأجل الضرورة، وتركوا في ذلك قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله بالنجاسة وأفتوا بقول زفر وحده في مسائل معدودة خمسة اهـ كلامه قدس سره والذي يقوي ما ذكره من عدم البعد في الفتوى بطهارة الأرواث ما قدمه عن المبتغى من التوسعة لأرباب الدواب وأنه رواية عن محمد أيضاً، ولا شك في الضرورة في هذه المسألة فتحتاج إلى التوسعة كما وسع على أرباب الدواب فإن الضرورة فيهم ليست بأشد مما هنا فإن أكثر المحلات مياهها قليلة وإن حياضها لا تكون ملأى دائماً والماء ينقطع تارة ويجيء أخرى، وفي غالب الأوقات يستصحب الماء عين الزبل ويعسر الاستعمال من غير هذا الماء سيما على النساء في بيوتهن فلا يمكنهن الخروج، وعند قطع الأنهر لكريها تشتد الضرورة إلى أن الحياض في أسفلها عين الزبل غالباً ويستمر انقطاعها أياماً وما جعل عليكم في الدين من حرج. قوله: (وألحقوا بالجاري حوض الحمام قال الرملي أقول : وبالأولى إلحاق الآبار المعينة ذلك
Then he quoted from Ibn Hajar al-Shafi'i in Sharh al-'Ubab based on the statement of Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him: 'When the matter becomes constrained, it becomes expansive'—that the change of the rivers of Syria due to the dung in them, even if little, does not harm because they cannot flow without it, which people are compelled to. End. He said: And it appears from his statement 'it does not harm, etc.' that what is pardoned according to him is the trace of the dung, not its substance. And all this is based on the impurity of dung according to us and according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him. Then he quoted the expressions of the jurists regarding that. The summary is that according to Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, dung and animal droppings are pure. And from Zufar: the dung of animals whose meat is eaten is pure, and also from him: absolutely, like Malik. Then he said: In the book al-Mubtagha (with ghayn): all dung is impure except a narration from Muhammad that it is pure due to widespread incidence. In this narration there is leniency for owners of animals, for rarely are they safe from being smeared with dung and droppings. So preserve this narration. End of the speech of al-Mubtagha. He said: And if you wish to follow (taqlid) one who says purity, then look at its conditions in the rest of the issue and act accordingly. And if we say that the fatwa is on the statement of Zufar regarding the purity of all dung with respect to the change of water in our land, then it is not far-fetched because necessity calls for that, just as our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them, gave fatwa on the statement of Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, regarding the purity of used water for necessity, and they abandoned in that the statement of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, regarding impurity, and gave fatwa on the statement of Zufar alone in five counted issues. End of his speech, may his secret be sanctified. And what strengthens what he mentioned about the lack of far-fetchedness in the fatwa of purity of dung is what he previously presented from al-Mubtagha regarding leniency for animal owners and that it is also a narration from Muhammad. And there is no doubt about the necessity in this issue, so it needs leniency just as leniency was granted to animal owners. For the necessity among them is not more severe than here, because most neighborhoods have little water, and their basins are not always full, and water sometimes stops and sometimes comes. And in most times, water carries the substance of dung, and it is difficult to use other than this water, especially for women in their homes, for they cannot go out. And when the rivers are cut off for dredging, the necessity becomes severe to the extent that the basins at their bottom have mostly the substance of dung, and their interruption continues for days. And He has not placed upon you any hardship in the religion. His statement: (And they included the bathhouse basin in the ruling of flowing water. Al-Ramli said: I say: and it is even more appropriate to include the designated wells—that.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the impurity (najasa) of animal dung (الأرواث والأخثاء). The author quotes a Shafi'i scholar, Ibn Hajar, who uses the maxim 'when matters become constrained, they become expansive' to permit the impurity of dung in rivers due to necessity. The author then contrasts this with the Hanafi position. He summarizes the opinions: Malik considers all dung pure. Zufar (a Hanafi) holds that dung of edible animals is pure, and in one narration, he considers all dung pure like Malik. The majority Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad in the apparent riwaya) is that dung is najis. However, a rare narration from Muhammad (رواية عن محمد) states that all dung is pure due to widespread incidence (للبلوى), and this narration provides leniency for animal owners. The author, following al-Mubtagha, recommends preserving this narration. He then argues for giving fatwa according to Zufar's opinion (all dung pure) for the necessity in their land where water is contaminated with dung, similar to how Hanafi scholars gave fatwa on Muhammad's opinion about used water despite the disagreement of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The author emphasizes the extreme necessity, especially for women who cannot leave their homes. He concludes by noting that even the bathhouse basin is included in the ruling of flowing water, and analogically, wells should be included as well.

Key Terms
الروث (al-rawth): Dung of animals (especially horses, donkeys, etc.), considered najis in the majority Hanafi view.
الخثى (al-khuthā): Animal droppings, particularly of cows and buffaloes, similar in ruling to روث.
البلوى (al-balwā): Widespread incidence or common occurrence, used as a basis for leniency in rulings (rukhsa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi scholars in this passage, which opinion on the purity of animal dung is deemed permissible to follow in cases of widespread necessity, even though it opposes the majority view?
A) The opinion of Imam Malik that all dung is pure.
B) The opinion of Zufar that all dung is pure.
C) The opinion of Abu Yusuf that dung is pure when dry.
D) The opinion of Imam Shafi'i that only horse dung is pure.

Key Takeaway
Within the Hanafi school, the impurity of animal dung is the majority view, but a rare narration from Muhammad and Zufar's opinion allow for purity, which may be followed as a fatwa in cases of necessity, particularly for water purity.

Ruling on Used Water and the Hammam Basin Analogy
Fatwa on used water and analogy to hammam basin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi fatwa on the purity of used water and explain the analogy of the hammam basin to flowing water, identifying the conditions for the basin water to be considered flowing.

Core Idea
Used water is pure in the later fatwa; a hammam basin is like flowing water if continuously replenished.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq resolves a key Hanafi dispute on used water and extends the ruling to the common case of the hammam basin. The author clarifies that while early Hanafis deemed used water impure, the fatwa has shifted to purity due to necessity, and the basin water is treated as flowing under certain conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute on used water
Recognize that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf held used water to be impure, while Imam Muhammad held it pure. The later fatwa follows Muhammad due to necessity.
The dispute hinges on whether used water exits the washing area before repeated use.
2
State the mufta bihi ruling
The fatwa is on absolute permissibility: used water is pure (tahir) and can be used for purification.
This ruling appears in Fath al-Qadir and al-Fatawa al-Sughra.
3
Analyze the qiyas to the hammam basin
Scholars analogized the hammam basin to flowing water. The basin water is considered jari if water descends from above and is continuously replenished, so impurities do not affect it.
The illa is the continuous flow, which prevents impurity from settling.
4
Examine the condition and exception
If the trace of impurity appears (e.g., dung), there is no pardon because necessity is absent (people can wait for water to clear). However, impurity settled in channels is pardoned when water flows over it clearly.
The author prefers the view that requires appearance of trace to declare water impure, making the pardon unnecessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خمساً في خمس اختلف فيه واختار السعدي جوازه، والخلاف مبني على أنه هل يخرج الماء المستعمل قبل تكرر الاستعمال إذا كان بهذه المساحة أو لا، وهذه مبنية على نجاسة المستعمل. كذا في فتح القدير. وقد قدمنا أن الفتوى على الجواز مطلقاً وكذا صرح في الفتاوى الصغرى، وألحقوا بالجاري حوض الحمام إذا كان الماء ينزل من أعلاه حتى لو أدخلت القصعة النجسة واليد النجسة فيه لا تتنجس. وهل يشترط مع ذلك تدارك اعتراف الناس سره: وظاهر كلام المصنف رحمه الله هنا أن العفو في ذلك كائن وإن ظهر أثر السرقين في الماء حملاً على التغير بالمكث ونحو ذلك مما فيه الضرورة والصواب ما ذكرناه أولاً أن أثر النجاسة إذا ظهر في الماء فلا عفو حينئذ لعدم الضرورة بانتظار صفو الماء غايته العفو عن النجاسة المستقرة في باطن القساطل إذا جرى الماء عليها صافياً على حسب ما قدمنا بيانه وعدم تنجيس الماء الطاهر بالزبل النجس للضرورة حيث لا يجري الماء إلا به لكونه يسد خروق القساطل فلا ينفذ الماء منها ويبقى جارياً فوقه اهـ . قلت : ولا يخفى أنه على القول باشتراط ظهور الأثر في الجاري يكون طاهراً فلا حاجة إلى القول بالعفو عنه بناء عليه.
Five on five disputed, and al-Sa'di chose its permissibility. The disagreement is based on whether the used water exits before repeated use if it is of that area or not. This is based on the impurity of used water. So in Fath al-Qadir. We have stated that the fatwa is on absolute permissibility, and likewise it is clarified in al-Fatawa al-Sughra. They analogized to flowing water the basin of the hammam when water descends from its top, so even if a impure bowl and impure hand are inserted into it, it does not become impure. Is it also required that the replenishment of people's acknowledgment? Its secret: The apparent statement of the author, may Allah have mercy on him, here is that the pardon in that is existent even if the trace of dung appears in the water, carrying it as change due to staying and the like where there is necessity. And the correct is what we mentioned first: when the trace of impurity appears in the water, then there is no pardon at that time because there is no necessity to wait for the water to clear. At most, there is pardon for the impurity settled inside the channels when water flows over it clear, as per our earlier explanation, and the non-impurification of pure water by impure dung due to necessity where water only flows with it because it blocks the holes of the channels, so water does not leak from them and remains flowing over it. End. I say: It is not hidden that according to the view requiring the appearance of the trace in flowing water, it is pure, so there is no need to speak of pardon based on that.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the ruling of used water (al-ma' al-musta'mal) and its status. The author begins by referencing a dispute regarding whether water becomes impure if it is used five times in an area of five cubits (a specific measure). Al-Sa'di chose the view of permissibility. The underlying difference is whether the used water exits the area before repeated use; if it exits, it might be considered pure, otherwise not. This hinges on the view of the impurity of used water. The author states that the fatwa is on absolute permissibility, meaning used water is pure, as mentioned in Fath al-Qadir and al-Fatawa al-Sughra. Then the author mentions that scholars analogized the basin of a public bath (hammam) to flowing water: if water falls from above into the basin, the basin water is considered flowing, so inserting an impure bowl or hand does not cause impurity. However, there is a condition: the water must be continuously replenished (tadarak) as people use it. The author then quotes a secret note: The author of the matn apparently considers that the water is pardoned even if the effect of dung appears in it, interpreting the change as due to stagnant water (makkth) where necessity exists. But the correct view is that if the trace of impurity appears in the water, there is no pardon because people can wait for the water to clear. The only pardon is for impurity settled inside the water channels (qasatil) when clear water flows over it, and for pure water not becoming impure by impure dung when necessity requires it to block leaks in channels. The commentator adds that according to the view that requires the trace of impurity in flowing water to make it impure, the water remains pure, so there is no need to speak of pardon.

Key Terms
الماء المستعمل (al-ma' al-musta'mal): Water that has been used for a valid ritual purification (wudu or ghusl).
الجاري (al-jari): Flowing water, such as a river or a spring, or a basin with continuous replenishment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa mentioned, what is the ruling on water that has been used for wudu?
A) It is impure and cannot be used again.
B) It is pure but not purifying.
C) It is pure and purifying.
D) It is impure unless it is flowing.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi fatwa considers used water pure, and the hammam basin is analogous to flowing water when continuously replenished, subject to the condition that trace of impurity does not appear.

Purity of Animal Dung: Concession for Necessity (Balwa)
Examining intra-Hanafi dispute on dung purity and fatwa of necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi positions on the purity of animal dung and explain how the principle of necessity (balwa/darura) leads to a fatwa of concession.

Core Idea
Animal dung is impure by default, but a narration of Imam Muhammad permits it due to widespread need — fatwa follows concession in times of necessity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into a practical fiqh issue: the purity of animal dung when it inevitably mixes with water in rivers and basins. While the default Hanafi position is that all dung is impure (najis), a rare narration from Imam Muhammad considers it pure due to widespread affliction (balwa). The author argues that necessity (darura) justifies adopting this concession, especially for communities where water scarcity and contamination are common.

Guided Path
1
State the default Hanafi ruling
Identify that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold all animal dung is impure (najis). This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
This is the standard ruling for daily practice.
2
Identify the intra-Hanafi variation
Note the narration from Imam Muhammad that dung is pure due to balwa (widespread affliction), and Zufar's view that dung of halal-meat animals is pure, or absolutely pure like Malik.
These are nawadir (rare transmissions).
3
Understand the principle of necessity
The Quranic principle 'No hardship in religion' (22:78) is invoked to allow following a rare narration when avoiding impurity causes extreme difficulty.
This mirrors the earlier fatwa on used water.
4
Apply the fatwa to the water change issue
The author extends the concession to water that changes due to dung in rivers and basins, especially when people cannot avoid using such water.
The fatwa is specific to conditions of necessity.
5
Summarize the combined position
The mufta bihi position in necessity cases is that dung is pure, following the narration from Muhammad and Zufar, replacing the default impurity ruling.
This is a tarjih (preference) based on need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقل عن ابن حجر الشافعي في شرح العباب بناء على قول الإمام الشافعي رحمه الله : إذا ضاق الأمر اتسع أنه لا يضر تغير أنهر الشام بما فيها من الزبل ولو قليلة لأنه لا يمكن جريها المضطر إليه الناس إلا به اهـ قال والظاهر من قوله «لا يضر الخ» أن المعفو عنه عنده أثر الزبل لا عينه وهذا كله بناء على نجاسة الزبل عندنا وعند الشافعي رحمه الله، ثم نقل عبارات الفقهاء في ذلك. وحاصلها أن الروث والختي عند مالك رحمه الله طاهران، وعن زفر روث ما يؤكل لحمه طاهر، وعنه أيضاً مطلقاً كمالك. ثم قال : وفي كتاب المبتغى بالغين المعجمة : الأرواث كلها نجسة إلا رواية عن محمد أنها طاهرة للبلوى وفي هذه الرواية توسعة لأرباب الدواب فقل ما يسلمون عن التلطخ بالأرواث والأخثاء فتحفظ هذه الرواية اهـ كلام المبتغى. قال: وإذا أردت تقليد من يقول بالطهارة فانظر في شروطه في باقي المسألة واعمل على ذلك. وإن قلنا بالفتوى على قول زفر في طهارة الأرواث كلها بالنسبة إلى تغير الماء في بلادنا هذه فلا يبعد لأن الضرورة داعية إلى ذلك كما أفتى علماؤنا رحمهم الله بقول محمد رحمه الله في طهارة الماء المستعمل لأجل الضرورة، وتركوا في ذلك قول أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمهما الله بالنجاسة وأفتوا بقول زفر وحده في مسائل معدودة خمسة اهـ كلامه قدس سره والذي يقوي ما ذكره من عدم البعد في الفتوى بطهارة الأرواث ما قدمه عن المبتغى من التوسعة لأرباب الدواب وأنه رواية عن محمد أيضاً، ولا شك في الضرورة في هذه المسألة فتحتاج إلى التوسعة كما وسع على أرباب الدواب فإن الضرورة فيهم ليست بأشد مما هنا فإن أكثر المحلات مياهها قليلة وإن حياضها لا تكون ملأى دائماً والماء ينقطع تارة ويجيء أخرى، وفي غالب الأوقات يستصحب الماء عين الزبل ويعسر الاستعمال من غير هذا الماء سيما على النساء في بيوتهن فلا يمكنهن الخروج، وعند قطع الأنهر لكريها تشتد الضرورة إلى أن الحياض في أسفلها عين الزبل غالباً ويستمر انقطاعها أياماً وما جعل عليكم في الدين من حرج.
Then he quoted from Ibn Hajar al-Shafi'i in Sharh al-Abab, based on the saying of Imam al-Shafi'i: 'When the matter becomes narrow, it becomes wide' that it does not harm the change of the rivers of Syria due to the dung in them, even if little, because people cannot make them flow except with it. He said: The apparent meaning of his words 'it does not harm...' is that what is pardoned according to him is the trace of dung, not its substance. All this is based on the impurity of dung according to us and according to al-Shafi'i. Then he quoted the statements of jurists on that. Their summary: dung (rawth) and mixed dung (khathi) according to Malik are pure. From Zufar, dung of animals whose meat is eaten is pure, and from him also absolutely, like Malik. He said: If you wish to follow someone who says purity, then look at its conditions in the rest of the issue and act accordingly. What strengthens his mention of not being far-fetched in the fatwa of purity of dung is what he presented from al-Mubtagha of expansion for animal owners and that it is also a narration from Muhammad. Indeed most localities have little water, their basins are not always full, water cuts off sometimes and comes another time. In most times, the water carries the actual dung, and it is difficult to use other than this water, especially for women in their homes, they cannot go out. Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on the purity of animal dung (arwath, pl. of rawth) and the fatwa of permissibility due to necessity. The author quotes from Ibn Hajar al-Shafi'i, who based on a principle from al-Shafi'i ('when the matter becomes narrow, it becomes wide') says that the change of Syrian rivers due to dung does not harm because people cannot avoid it. However, the pardon is for the trace of dung, not the substance itself. Then the author summarizes the views of various scholars: Imam Malik considers all dung pure; Zufar considers dung of animals whose meat is eaten pure, and in one narration absolutely like Malik. The author then quotes from the book al-Mubtagha, which states that all dung is impure except a narration from Imam Muhammad that it is pure due to widespread affliction (balwa). This narration provides expansion for animal owners who can hardly avoid being smeared with dung. The author suggests that if one wants to follow the view of purity, one should look into its conditions. He further notes that the fatwa on the purity of all dung regarding the change of water in our lands is not far-fetched because necessity demands it, just as earlier Hanafi scholars gave fatwa on Imam Muhammad's view regarding used water due to necessity, leaving the views of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. This is further supported by the narration from Muhammad in al-Mubtagha. The author emphasizes the necessity in this issue, especially in localities where water is scarce and often carries dung, making it difficult for people, especially women, to avoid using such water. The principle of 'no hardship in religion' is invoked.

Key Terms
الأرواث (al-arwath): Plural of rawth (روث); animal dung, feces of beasts of burden.
البلوى (al-balwa): Widespread affliction or a common calamity that makes it difficult to avoid impurity.
الضرورة (al-darura): Necessity that permits normally prohibited actions or rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) وَيُحِلُّ لَهُمُ الطَّيِّبَاتِ
C) فَأَيْنَمَا تُوَلُّوا فَثَمَّ وَجْهُ اللَّهِ
D) وَلَا تُلْقُوا بِأَيْدِيكُمْ إِلَى التَّهْلُكَةِ

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the default impurity of animal dung can be overridden by a fatwa of purity when necessity demands it, based on a rare narration from Imam Muhammad and the principle of no hardship.
