Hammam Basin: When Is It Like Flowing Water?
Detailed conditions for hammam basin to be treated as flowing water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for when a hammam basin is considered flowing water and explain the intra-madhhab dispute about the requirement of a continuous source (madd).

Core Idea
A hammam basin is like flowing water if scooping is continuous, but scholars differ on whether a constant source is needed.

In daily life, water in a hammam basin is frequently used for wudu and ghusl. Knowing whether this water remains pure and purifying after use or becomes used (musta'mal) is essential for valid purification. This lesson unpacks the conditions set by Hanafi jurists for analogizing the hammam basin to flowing water, and explores a key internal dispute about the necessity of a continuous source. By the end, you will understand the operative ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Analogy
The matn states that the hammam basin is analogous to flowing water (jari). Identify what 'flowing water' legally means in the Hanafi school: water that moves continuously and does not become impure unless its properties change.
Flowing water is defined by movement, not necessarily by having a source.
2
Understand the Condition of Continuous Scooping
The key condition for the analogy is that the scooping (gharf) must be continuous (mutadarak), meaning the water's surface never becomes still between two scoops. This ensures the water is in motion, analogous to a river.
Tafseer of gharf: the water surface should not settle.
3
Examine the Dispute on Source (Madd)
Some Hanafi scholars (e.g., Fath al-Qadir) require the water to have a continuous source (madd) like a spring or river. Others (e.g., al-Siraj al-Wahhaj) hold that continuous movement suffices, even without madd. Locate the evidence cited for each view.
The dispute affects whether water collected in a secondary pit remains pure.
4
Evaluate the Evidence and Tarjih
Analyze the proofs: the story of Imam al-Zahid about three successive canals and the case of a dammed river (Waqi'at al-Natifi) support the view that madd is not required. The tarjih (preferred view) in the madhhab is that madd is not a condition.
Al-Siraj al-Wahhaj declared the view without madd as 'sahih' (correct).
5
Apply the Ruling and Note Practical Consequences
Based on the chosen view, water in a hammam basin with continuous scooping is like flowing water: it does not become used or impure unless it changes color, taste, or smell. This applies even if the water collects in a secondary receptacle, as long as the scooping is continuous.
The earlier ruling that second-pit water is spoiled is based on the view that used water is impure, which is not the relied-upon position.

His saying: (And they analogized to flowing water the basin of the hammam.) Al-Ramli said: I say: and with greater reason, the specific wells? There is a dispute about that, mentioned in al-Munyah and al-Mujtaba: The most correct is that if water enters through the pipe and the scooping is continuous (mutadarak), it is like flowing water. The interpretation of the scooping (gharf) is that the face of the water does not become still between two scoopings. In Fath al-Qadir he said: Then it is necessary that its flow be from a source (madd) like a spring and a river; that is the chosen view. End. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: It is not required for flowing water that it have a source (madd), and that is the correct view. End. In al-Tanjis, al-Mi'raj, and others: If a flowing river is dammed from above, and a person makes wudu with the water flowing in the river while the water continues to flow, it is permissible because this is flowing water. End. This testifies to what is in al-Siraj. Al-Siraj al-Hindi mentioned from Imam al-Zahid that if a person digs a canal from a small basin and causes water to flow in the canal and makes wudu with that water while it is flowing, then that water collects in a place and settles, then another person digs a canal from that place and causes water to flow in it and makes wudu with it while it is flowing, then that water collects in another place as well, then a third person does likewise, the wudu of all is permissible because each of them made wudu with the water when it was flowing, and flowing water does not accept impurity unless it changes. From al-Hasan ibn Ziyad there is evidence indicating the impermissibility of wudu with the second and third water. For he said about two pits, one exiting and entering the other, and he made wudu from the spout between them: it is permissible, but the pit into which water enters becomes spoiled. And if he has a wide spout and a vessel of water that he needs, and he is hopeful of finding water but not certain, what should he do? It is narrated from Shaykh al-Zahid Abu al-Hasan al-Rustughfani that he used to say: He orders one of his companions that he pours water at one end, makes wudu in it, and at the other end of the spout a vessel collects the water; the collected water is pure and purifying because its use occurred during its flow, and flowing water does not become used by its being used. Some mashayikh rejected this statement and said: Flowing water only does not become used if it has a source (madd) like a spring or river; if it does not have a source, it becomes used. The correct view is the first one, based on the issue of Waqi'at al-Natifi: if a river is dammed from above and a person makes wudu with what flows, it is permissible because there is no source left for the water, yet it is permissible to make wudu with it. End of what al-Siraj al-Hindi mentioned. And know that it has preceded from Fath that their statement 'what collects in the second pit is spoiled' and many similar things are based on the impurity of used water. So according to the chosen view of its purity, it is not, so let this be preserved to branch upon, and one should not give fatwa with such branches. Branches: In al-Khulasa, attributed to al-Asl: One may make wudu from a basin in which one fears impurity but is not certain, and he is not obliged to ask unless he needs it when there is no evidence; and the original principle is that it permits use. And Umar, may Allah be pleased with him, said when he asked Amr ibn al-Aas, the owner of the basin: 'Do the wild animals return to it, O...' the one with the waterwheel in our country, since water springs from its bottom and the scoops with buckets are continuous (mutadarak), superior to the replenishment of the hammam basin, so there is no doubt that the ruling of its water is that of flowing water. So if during the rotation something falls...
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on the ruling of the water in a hammam basin and its analogy to flowing water. It begins by quoting the matn: 'And they analogized to flowing water the basin of the hammam.' Al-Ramli comments that this analogy applies even more to wells (abi muiyana) in our lands where water springs from the bottom and is drawn by scoops continuously. There is a dispute about the condition for the basin to be like flowing water: the correct view, as in al-Munyah and al-Mujtaba, is that if water enters through a pipe and the scooping (gharf) is continuous—meaning the water's surface does not become still between two scoopings—then it is like flowing water. The author then quotes different views on whether a continuous source (madd) is required. In Fath al-Qadir, the chosen view is that the water must have a source or be like a spring or river. However, in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, it is stated that a source is not required, and this is considered correct. Evidence is brought from al-Tanjis, etc., that if a river is dammed from above and water still flows, wudu with that flowing water is permissible even though the source is cut off. This supports the view of al-Siraj. Then, al-Siraj al-Hindi narrates a story from Imam al-Zahid about three men digging successive canals from a small basin; all three made wudu while the water was flowing, and all were valid because flowing water does not become impure unless it changes. However, al-Hasan ibn Ziyad's view indicates that the second and third water becomes spoiled (i.e., used water) if it collects in a pit. The author then mentions a dispute about whether flowing water becomes used (musta'mal) by being used. Some mashayikh said it becomes used if it has no source; but the correct view is that it does not become used as long as it is flowing, based on the precedent from Waqi'at al-Natifi. The author concludes that the statements about the second pit being spoiled are based on the earlier view that used water is impure. According to the chosen view that used water is pure (from the first lesson), these rulings do not apply. One should not give fatwa with such branches. Finally, there are branches: in al-Khulasa, one may make wudu from a basin where impurity is feared but not certain, and one is not obliged to ask unless necessary. Umar's statement with Amr ibn al-Aas is cited to show that a basin with continuous scooping is like flowing water.

Key Terms
الغرف (al-gharf): The act of scooping water; the continuity of scooping (tadarak al-gharf) determines whether water is considered flowing.
المدد (al-madd): A continuous source of water, such as a spring or river, that supplies the flow.
الجاري (al-jari): Flowing water; water that moves continuously, either naturally or by human action.
الراكد (al-rakid): Standing water; water that is not flowing and becomes impure upon contact with impurities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct (sahih) view in the Hanafi madhhab, what is the minimum condition for water in a hammam basin to be considered flowing (jari)?
A) It must have a continuous source (madd) like a spring.
B) The scooping (gharf) must be continuous so the water surface never becomes still.
C) The basin must be at least 10 meters deep.
D) The water must be changed every hour.

Key Takeaway
Water in a hammam basin with continuous scooping is treated as flowing water, and it does not become used or impure even without a constant source.

Flowing Water Without a Continuous Source: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Examines whether flowing water requires a continuous source to avoid becoming musta'mal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on flowing water without a continuous source and state the mufta bihi, distinguishing it from the opposing intra-Hanafi view.

Core Idea
Flowing water remains tahur even without a source; flow itself, not madd, is the condition.

Does water need a continuous spring or river to remain pure after use? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether flowing water (ma' jar) must have a continuous source (madd) to avoid becoming musta'mal (used water). The selected ruling, supported by the case of a dammed river, holds that as long as water flows, it stays tahur. Understanding this illuminates how later Hanafi jurists resolved this practical question and how the underlying view of musta'mal water affects derived rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Pinpoint the legal question: does flowing water require a continuous source (like a spring or river) to prevent it from becoming musta'mal when used for wudu?
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; both positions are found in classical Hanafi texts.
2
State the two positions
First position (Fath al-Qadir): a source is required. Second position (al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, al-Siraj al-Hindi): no source is needed; flow alone suffices.
The first view is attributed to some early scholars like al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.
3
Examine the key evidence
The dammed river case: if a river is blocked from above but water still flows, wudu with that water is permissible even without a source. This directly supports the second view.
This case is cited from Waqi'at al-Natafi and is taken as decisive by later scholars.
4
Understand the tarjih mechanism
The second view is mufta bihi because it aligns with the chosen view that musta'mal water is tahir (not najis). The opposing view's rulings (e.g., second reservoir becomes invalid) rest on the assumption that musta'mal is najis.
Knowing the underlying usul (musta'mal's status) clarifies why the preferred position wins.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منه؟ فيه خلاف ذكره في المنية وفي المجتبى: الأصح أنه إن كان يدخل الماء في الأنبوب والغرف متدارك فهو كالجاري وتفسير الغرف أن لا يسكن وجه الماء فيما بين الغرفتين. قال في فتح القدير : ثم لا بد من كون جريانه لمد دلة كما في العين والنهر هو المختار ا هـ. وفي السراج الوهاج ولا يشترط في الماء الجاري المدد وهو الصحيح ا.هـ. وفي التنجيس والمعراج وغيرهما : الماء الجاري إذا سدّ من فوق فتوضأ إنسان بما يجري في النهر وقد بقي جري الماء كان جائزاً لأن هذا ماء جار ا.هـ. فهذا يشهد لما في السراج. وذكر السراج الهندي عن الإمام الزاهد أن من حفر نهراً من حوض صغير وأجرى الماء في النهر وتوضأ بذلك الماء في حال جريانه فاجتمع ذلك الماء في مكان واستقر فيه فحفر رجل آخر نهراً من ذلك المكان وأجرى الماء فيه وتوضأ به في حال جريانه فاجتمع ذلك الماء في مكان آخر أيضاً ففعل رجل ثالث كذلك، جاز وضوء الكل لأن كل واحد منهم إنما توضأ بالماء حال جريانه والماء الجاري لا يحتمل النجاسة ما لم يتغير وعن الحسن بن زياد ما يدل على عدم جواز وضوء الماء الثاني والثالث فانه قال في حفيرتين يخرج أحدهما ويدخل في الأخرى فتوضأ فيما بينهما جاز، والحفيرة التي يدخل فيها الماء تفسد، وإذا كان معه ميزاب واسع ومعه إداوة من ماء يحتاج إليه وهو على طمع من وجود الماء ولكن لا يتيقن ذلك ماذا يصنع ، حكي عن الشيخ الزاهد أبي الحسن الرستغفني أنه كان يقول : يأمر أحد رفقائه أنه الماء في طرف يصب . يتوضأ فيه وعند الطرف الآخر من الميزاب إناء يجتمع فيه الماء فالمجتمع طاهر وطهور لأن استعماله حصل في حال جريانه والماء الجاري لا يصير مستعملاً باستعماله. ومن المشايخ من أنكر هذا القول وقال : الماء الجاري إنما لا يصير مستعملاً إذا كان له مدد كالعين والنهر، أما إذا لم يكن له مدد يصير مستعملاً، والصحيح القول الأول بدليل مسألة واقعات الناطفي أن النهر إذا سد من فوق فتوضأ إنسان بما يجري فإنه يجوز فإن هناك لم يبق للماء مدد ومع هذا يجوز التوضؤ به . ا هـ ما ذكره السراج الهندي واعلم أنه قد تقدم عن فتح أن قولهم ما اجتمع في الحفيرة الثانية فاسدة وكذا كثير من أشباه ذلك إنما هو بناء على نجاسة الماء المستعمل، فأما على المختار من طهارته فلا فلتحفظ ليفرع عليها ولا يفتي بمثل هذه الفروع.
Regarding it? There is a difference of opinion mentioned in al-Munya and al-Mujtaba: the most correct is that if water enters the pipe and the scoops are consecutive, it is like flowing water. The interpretation of 'scoops' is that the surface of the water does not become still between the two scoops. Said in Fath al-Qadir: Then it is necessary that its flow be due to a continuous source, as in a spring and river; that is the chosen view. In al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: A continuous source is not required for flowing water, and that is the correct view. In al-Tanjis, al-Mi'raj, and others: If flowing water is dammed from above, and a person performs wudu with what flows in the river and water flow remains, it is permissible because this is flowing water. This testifies to what is in al-Siraj. Al-Siraj al-Hindi mentioned from Imam al-Zahid that a person dug a canal from a small basin and made water flow in the canal and performed wudu with that water while it was flowing, then that water collected in a place and settled, then another person dug a canal from that place and made water flow in it and performed wudu with it while flowing, then that water collected in another place, then a third person did likewise; the wudu of all is permissible because each of them performed wudu with water while it was flowing, and flowing water does not retain impurity as long as it has not changed. From al-Hasan ibn Ziyad is evidence indicating the impermissibility of the wudu of the second and third water; he said regarding two reservoirs, one emptying into the other: if one performs wudu with the water between them, it is permissible, but the reservoir into which the water enters becomes invalid. If one has a wide spout and a small container of water he needs, and he has hope of finding water but is not certain, what should he do? It is narrated from Shaykh al-Zahid Abu al-Hasan al-Rustaghfani that he used to say: He should order one of his companions to pour water at one end; he performs wudu with it, and at the other end of the spout there is a vessel that collects the water, and the collected water is tahir and tahur because its use occurred while it was flowing, and flowing water does not become musta'mal by being used. Some scholars rejected this statement and said: Flowing water only does not become musta'mal if it has a continuous source, like a spring or river; if it does not have a continuous source, it becomes musta'mal. The correct view is the first, based on the case of the Waqi'at al-Natafi that if a river is dammed from above and a person performs wudu with what flows, it is permissible; there, the water has no source, yet wudu with it is permissible. End of what al-Siraj al-Hindi mentioned. Know that it preceded from Fath that their statement that what collects in the second reservoir is invalid, and many similar statements, are only based on the impurity of musta'mal water. But according to the chosen view that it is tahir, [those rulings do not apply]; so let it be memorized for branching rulings, and one should not give fatwa with such branches.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the condition of flowing water (ma' jar) for wudu. The central issue: does flowing water need a continuous source (madd) to prevent it from becoming musta'mal (used water) when used for wudu? The passage begins by citing a difference of opinion. According to al-Munya and al-Mujtaba, the most correct view is that water in a pipe with continuous scoops (ghurf) is like flowing water. Fath al-Qadir states that a source is required, which is the chosen view there. However, al-Siraj al-Wahhaj declares that a source is not required, and that is the correct view. Evidence for the latter comes from the case of a river dammed from above: wudu is permissible with the remaining flow even though the source is cut off. Al-Siraj al-Hindi then narrates a scenario: a person digs a canal from a small basin; all three persons who perform wudu from the successive collections while the water is flowing are valid. This is because flowing water does not become najis or musta'mal unless it changes. Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad's transmission suggests that the water in the second reservoir becomes invalid (fāsid), but the commentator clarifies that this is based on the view that musta'mal water is najis. However, the mufta bihi (operative view) is that musta'mal water is tahir (pure), so those rulings are not to be given fatwa upon. The correct view is thus the first: flowing water does not become musta'mal by use, even without a source, as long as it is flowing.

Key Terms
ماء جار (ma' jar): Flowing water; water that is moving on its own or by a cause, which does not retain impurity unless changed.
مدد (madd): Continuous source or supply of water, like a spring or river.
مستعمل (musta'mal): Used water, i.e., water that has been used for wudu or ghusl. There is ikhtilaf on whether it is ṭāhir or najis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred opinion in this passage, if a river is dammed from above and water still flows, is it permissible to perform wudu with that flowing water?
A) No, because the source is cut off and the water becomes musta'mal.
B) Yes, because the water is still flowing even without a source.
C) No, because the water might have changed.
D) Yes, because all water is permissible unless proven otherwise.

Key Takeaway
Flowing water does not require a continuous source to remain ṭahūr; flow alone prevents it from becoming musta'mal according to the preferred Hanafi view.

The Atomist Basis for Wudu from a Basin
Explains the atomist doctrine to justify the fiqh ruling on basin water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water in a basin after partial use and explain the atomist proof that supports it.

Core Idea
Water particles are discrete; using some does not make the whole musta'mal.

How can you perform wudu from a basin and still have the remaining water usable? The answer lies in classical kalam: bodies are made of finite indivisible atoms. This lesson unpacks the rational argument used by Hanafi scholars to justify a practical fiqh ruling against the Mu'tazila objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling in Question
Recognize that the issue is whether using water from a basin for wudu renders all the water in the basin musta'mal (used). The Hanafi ruling states only the water that contacts the limb becomes musta'mal; the rest remains pure.
2
Understand the Opposing View
The Mu'tazila argued that water is a continuous substance. If any part becomes musta'mal, the entire mass becomes musta'mal. This follows from their hyle+form ontology.
3
Learn the Atomist Doctrine
Ahl al-Sunnah hold that all bodies are composed of indivisible atoms (juz' la yatajazza'). These atoms are discrete and finite. Water is an aggregation of such particles, not a true continuum.
4
Connect to the Fiqh Ruling
Since water atoms are separate, only the atoms that touch the limb become musta'mal. The remaining atoms are unaffected. Thus the basin remains pure for further use.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) supporting a transmitted ruling.
5
Reflect on the Wider Implications
The atomist view also explains resurrection: Allah reassembles the same atoms. It prevents infinite regress and aligns with definitive texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : قيل مسألة الحوض بناء على الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ الخ) بيان ذلك كما في شرح الهداية لسيدنا الأستاذ عبد الغني أن الأجسام المركبة كالماء والحجر ونحوهما، هم يقولون إنها مركبة من الهيولي وهي المادة الكلية ومن الصورة الجزئي فقط فيلزم على هذا أن يكون ماء الحوض كله على مذهبهم متصلاً واحداً، فلو توضأ فيه صار لكونه شيئاً واحداً وهو باطل، فإن مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة نصر ا كلمتهم إلى قيام الساعة أن الأجسام كلها مركبة من الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لا وهماً ولا فرض كما قرر في موضعه من علم الكلام، وهو أربعة أنواع في كل جسم مركب أي جسم كان: نوع من النار، ونوع من الهواء، ونوع من الماء، ونوعه من التراب. فإذا أراد الله تعالى تركيب جسم من الأجسام جمع بيد قدرته من كل نوع من هذه الأنواع الأربعة أجزاء صغاراً متلاصقة وضم بعضها إلى بعض بتدبير إلهي خاص فتكون جسماً، ثم إذا أراد إعدام ذلك الجسم فرق بين أنواعه فيذهب كل نوع من تلك الأجزاء إلى جنسه، ثم إذا كان يوم القيامة أعاد تلك الأجزاء إلى ما كانت عليه من التركيب البعث الذي وردت به النصوص القطعية، ثم إن كل نوع من هذه الأنواع الأربعة مركب أيضاً من أجزاء صغار لا تحتمل القسمة متلاصقة يشبه بعضها بعضاً بحيث تظهر كالشيء الواحد فتتصل وتنقطع لشدة مناسبة بعضها لبعض ولكن لا تشبه أجزاء هذا النوع أجزاء النوع الآخر، فالماء أجزاء صغار متلاصقة متناسبة يتصل بعضها ببعض وينفصل بعضها عن بعض، وكذلك الهواء والنار والتراب، فلو توضأ أحد بالماء حتى صار بعض تلك الأجزاء مستعملاً لا يلزم أن تصير بقية الأجزاء مستعملة كذلك لأن الماء عندنا ليس شيئاً واحداً إلا بحسب ظاهر الصورة التركيبية الحاصلة من اجتماع الأجزاء الصغار التي لا تتجزأ، وإنما هو مركب من أجزاء متناهية تنفصل وتتصل فلا يلزم استعمال الجميع بل البعض. والحق أن الأجزاء في كل مركب متناهية كما هو مذهب أهل السنة وإلا لزم أن يدخل ما لا نهاية له في الوجود وهو باطل بإجماع العقلاء كما ثبت بذلك بطلان التسلسل
His statement: It is said the issue of the basin is based on the indivisible atom. Explanation of that, as in Sharh al-Hidayah by our master Abdul Ghani: Composite bodies like water, stone, etc., they (the opponents) say they are composed of hyle (universal matter) and particular form only. This would entail that the entire basin's water in their view is connected as one. So if one performed wudu in it, it would all become musta'mal because it is one thing—this is false. Indeed, the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah—may Allah support their word until the Hour—is that all bodies are composed of the indivisible atom, not conceptually or hypothetically, as established in its place in kalam. It is of four types in every composite body, whatever body it is: a type of fire, a type of air, a type of water, and a type of earth. When Allah desires to compose a body, He gathers with His power from each of these four types small contiguous parts and joins them together with special divine planning, so it becomes a body. Then when He desires to annihilate that body, He separates its types, so each type of those parts goes to its genus. Then on the Day of Resurrection, He returns those parts to the composition they were, the resurrection established by definitive texts. Then each of these four types is also composed of small parts that do not admit division, contiguous, resembling each other such that they appear as one thing, connecting and separating due to the strong similarity between them, but parts of one type do not resemble parts of another type. So water is small contiguous proportionate parts, some connecting with others and some separating from others, likewise air, fire, and earth. So if someone performs wudu with water until some of those parts become musta'mal, it does not necessitate that the remaining parts become musta'mal as well, because water is not one thing except in the apparent form of composition resulting from the aggregation of small indivisible parts. Rather, it is composed of finite parts that separate and connect, so use of all is not necessitated, only some. The truth is that parts in every composite are finite, as is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunnah; otherwise, what is infinite would enter existence, which is false by consensus of rationalists, as proven by the impossibility of infinite regress.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a theological exposition defending the Hanafi ruling that water in a basin does not all become musta'mal when some is used. The context is a refutation of the Mu'tazilite position, which is implicitly referenced as 'they say' (qil). The commentator, quoting Sharh al-Hidayah, explains that the Mu'tazila hold that bodies are composed of hyle (universal matter) and particular form only, implying that the entire body of water is one continuous substance. Thus, if one part is used, the whole becomes musta'mal. The Ahl al-Sunnah (including Hanafis) counter that bodies are composed of indivisible atoms (juz' la yatajazza'), which are finite, discrete particles. Water is a composite of such atoms, and they are not actually connected as one; they only appear as a continuous mass. Therefore, using some atoms does not affect the others. The author further elaborates on the four elements (fire, air, water, earth) and how Allah combines them. The practical ruling: if some atoms become musta'mal, the rest remain pure. This kalam argument is used to validate the fiqh ruling that wudu from a basin does not render the entire basin impure or invalid for further use. The passage ends by affirming the finiteness of parts in composites and rejecting infinite regress, which is essential to the atomist doctrine.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'): The indivisible atom; the smallest particle of matter that cannot be further divided physically or hypothetically.
هيولي (hayula): Hyle; universal matter, the substratum that receives form, as held by philosophers and Mu'tazila.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the atomist doctrine of Ahl al-Sunnah, if a person performs wudu from a basin, what happens to the remaining water?
A) The entire basin becomes musta'mal because water is one continuous substance.
B) Only the atoms that were used become musta'mal; the rest remain pure.
C) The water becomes najis and cannot be used.
D) The basin becomes impure until cleaned.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling that basin water remains ṭahūr after partial use is rationally supported by the Ahl al-Sunnah atomist doctrine, which views bodies as composites of discrete, finite particles.

The Basin Ruling and the Indivisible Atom Basis
Explains the Hanafi ruling on basin water purity based on atomistic physics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on basin water purity and explain how the doctrine of the indivisible atom (juz' la yatajazza') supports it.

Core Idea
Water is composed of separable atoms; only the atom contacting impurity becomes impure, not the whole basin.

You are likely familiar with the concept of 'used water' (musta'mal) in taharah. But what happens when only a part of a large basin of water becomes used or contacts impurity? The Hanafi school, grounded in the Ash'ari-Maturidi atomistic cosmology, holds that the remaining water remains pure. This lesson unpacks the theological and legal reasoning behind this ruling, contrasting it with the views of the Mu'tazila and philosophers.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify exactly what the Hanafi school rules regarding basin water: the water is composed of indivisible parts; only the part that directly contacts impurity becomes impure (musta'mal); the rest remains pure (tahir) and can be used.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Mu'tazila and philosophers argue that water is a single continuous substance; any impurity in one part makes the entire body of water impure because there is no real separation between parts.
This is the key point of disagreement (mukhtalaf fih).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand the opponent's rational proof: they deny the existence of the indivisible atom, so water is a continuum; part and whole are not distinct in a real sense.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is based on the kalam doctrine of the indivisible atom (juz' la yatajazza'). Bodies are composed of finite, separable atoms. The impossibility of infinite regress proves finitude. Therefore, parts are distinct; one part being used does not affect others.
This is the core of the talil (rational argument) in the passage.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: In a Hanafi mosque, a person can use water from one side of a large basin even if the other side was used for wudu. According to the opponents, the entire basin would become impure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : قيل مسألة الحوض بناء على الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ الخ) بيان ذلك كما في شرح الهداية لسيدنا الأستاذ عبد الغني أن الأجسام المركبة كالماء والحجر ونحوهما، هم يقولون إنها مركبة من الهيولي وهي المادة الكلية ومن الصورة الجزئي فقط فيلزم على هذا أن يكون ماء الحوض كله على مذهبهم متصلاً واحداً، فلو توضأ فيه صار لكونه شيئاً واحداً وهو باطل، فإن مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة نصر ا كلمتهم إلى قيام الساعة أن الأجسام كلها مركبة من الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لا وهماً ولا فرض كما قرر في موضعه من علم الكلام، وهو أربعة أنواع في كل جسم مركب أي جسم كان: نوع من النار، ونوع من الهواء، ونوع من الماء، ونوعه من التراب. فإذا أراد الله تعالى تركيب جسم من الأجسام جمع بيد قدرته من كل نوع من هذه الأنواع الأربعة أجزاء صغاراً متلاصقة وضم بعضها إلى بعض بتدبير إلهي خاص فتكون جسماً، ثم إذا أراد إعدام ذلك الجسم فرق بين أنواعه فيذهب كل نوع من تلك الأجزاء إلى جنسه، ثم إذا كان يوم القيامة أعاد تلك الأجزاء إلى ما كانت عليه من التركيب البعث الذي وردت به النصوص القطعية، ثم إن كل نوع من هذه الأنواع الأربعة مركب أيضاً من أجزاء صغار لا تحتمل القسمة متلاصقة يشبه بعضها بعضاً بحيث تظهر كالشيء الواحد فتتصل وتنقطع لشدة مناسبة بعضها لبعض ولكن لا تشبه أجزاء هذا النوع أجزاء النوع الآخر، فالماء أجزاء صغار متلاصقة متناسبة يتصل بعضها ببعض وينفصل بعضها عن بعض، وكذلك الهواء والنار والتراب، فلو توضأ أحد بالماء حتى صار بعض تلك الأجزاء مستعملاً لا يلزم أن تصير بقية الأجزاء مستعملة كذلك لأن الماء عندنا ليس شيئاً واحداً إلا بحسب ظاهر الصورة التركيبية الحاصلة من اجتماع الأجزاء الصغار التي لا تتجزأ، وإنما هو مركب من أجزاء متناهية تنفصل وتتصل فلا يلزم استعمال الجميع بل البعض. والحق أن الأجزاء في كل مركب متناهية كما هو مذهب أهل السنة وإلا لزم أن يدخل ما لا نهاية له في الوجود وهو باطل بإجماع العقلاء كما ثبت بذلك بطلان التسلسل جميعه وهذا مستعملاً هو عندهم ينجسه والماء له فيه كالبق والذباب والزنبور والعقرب والسمك والضفدع والسرطان لا تجزئته فيكون باقي الحوض طاهراً، وعند المعتزلة والفلاسفة هو معدوم فيكون كل الماء مجاوراً للنجاسة فيكون الحوض نجساً عندهم وقيل في هذا التقرير نظر ا هـ.
He said: It is said the issue of the basin is based on the indivisible atom... Explanation as in Sharh al-Hidayah by our master Abdul Ghani: That composite bodies like water, stone, etc., they say are composed of matter (universal substance) and form only, so it follows that on their school all the water of the basin is contiguous and one; so if one performs wudu in it, it becomes (impure) because it is one thing, which is false. Indeed, the school of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah (may Allah support their word until the Day of Judgment) is that all bodies are composed of the indivisible atom, not in imagination nor assumption, as established in its place in theology. And it is of four types in every composite body: a type of fire, a type of air, a type of water, and a type of earth. When Allah wills to compose a body, He gathers with His power small adjacent parts from each of these four types and joins them together with special divine arrangement, and it becomes a body. Then when He wills to annihilate that body, He separates its types, so each type of those parts goes to its genus. Then on the Day of Resurrection, He returns those parts to the composition they had (the resurrection established by definitive texts). Then each of these four types is also composed of small parts that cannot be divided, adjacent, resembling each other, appearing as one thing, joining and separating due to strong proportion among them, but parts of one type do not resemble parts of another type. Water is small adjacent proportional parts, some joining and separating; likewise air, fire, earth. So if one performs wudu with water until some of those parts become used, it does not necessitate that the remaining parts become likewise used, because water in our view is not one thing except in its apparent composite form resulting from the assembly of small indivisible parts; rather, it is composed of finite parts that separate and join, so not all are used, only some. The truth is that parts in every composite are finite as per Ahl al-Sunnah; otherwise, the infinite would enter existence, which is false by consensus of rational people, as the impossibility of regress is established. All of this is used water is with them? The water has in it like flies, mosquitoes, hornets, scorpions, fish, frogs, and crabs? It does not purify? So the rest of the basin remains pure. According to the Mu'tazila and philosophers, it is nonexistent, so all the water is adjacent to impurity, so the basin is impure in their view. And it is said there is consideration in this exposition.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute explaining the Hanafi ruling that water in a basin remains pure even if some part becomes used, based on the doctrine of the indivisible atom (juz' la yatajazza). The author expands on the matn's reference to this theological basis. He begins by quoting an explanation from Sharh al-Hidayah: composite bodies like water and stone are made of four elements (fire, air, water, earth), each composed of tiny indivisible parts. Water is a collection of these small parts that can separate and join. If some of these parts become used (musta'mal), the rest do not automatically become used because water is not a single continuous substance but an aggregate of discrete parts. Therefore, the remaining water in the basin is pure. This contrasts with the Mu'tazila and philosophers who hold that water is a single continuous entity, so any impurity in one part makes the whole basin impure. The Hanafi position is rooted in the Ash'ari/Maturidi theology of atoms, emphasizing that parts are finite and separable. The passage also notes that animals without flowing blood (like flies, etc.) do not contaminate the water when they die in it, which is a separate ruling but inserted here. The final comment 'there is consideration in this exposition' suggests some scholars found the theological frame problematic.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza'): The indivisible atom in Islamic theology (kalam), the smallest particle that cannot be further divided.
المستعمل (al-musta'mal): Water that has been used for wudu or ghusl; in Hanafi fiqh, it is considered pure but not purifying (tahir ghayr mutahhir).
الحوض (al-hawd): A basin or pool of water, typically not flowing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does water in a basin remain pure if only some of its parts become used?
A) Because water is a single continuous substance
B) Because water is composed of indivisible parts that are separate
C) Because the basin is large enough to dilute impurities
D) Because used water is considered impure and cannot mix

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling that basin water remains pure when only some parts are used is rooted in the kalam doctrine of the indivisible atom, distinguishing it from the continuous-substance view of the Mu'tazila.

Death of Animals Without Flowing Blood Does Not Contaminate Water
Ruling statement with textual expansion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the death of animals without flowing blood in water and distinguish between the two categories of animals covered by this rule.

Core Idea
Only animals with flowing blood make water impure when they die in it.

A common worry is that any dead animal in water makes it impure. The Hanafi school draws a clear distinction: only animals with 'flowing blood' (nafs sā'ilah) cause contamination. This lesson unpacks that ruling and explains why even aquatic animals like fish are exempt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate in the matn phrase
Look at the matn: 'موت ما لا دم له فيه لا ينجسه' — death of an animal without blood in water does not contaminate it. The predicate is the ruling (water remains pure).
The phrase 'ما لا دم له' includes both land insects and aquatic animals.
2
Recognize the two sub-rules from Al-Hidayah
Al-Hidayah splits this into two: (1) land animals without flowing blood (e.g., flies, scorpions), and (2) aquatic animals (e.g., fish, frogs). The matn combines them for brevity.
Both categories share the characteristic of lacking flowing blood, so the ruling is the same.
3
Understand why the matn combined them
The author of Kanz al-Daqā'iq merged the two rules into one phrase 'موت ما لا له' (death of what has no blood) for conciseness.
This emphasizes the single underlying criterion: absence of flowing blood.
4
Note the objection about aquatic animals
A critic objects that aquatic animals like fish do have blood, so the combined phrase might be imprecise. The commentator acknowledges this but leaves it unresolved.
The objection does not affect the practical ruling; aquatic animals are still considered without flowing blood in the legal sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وموت ما لا دم له فيه كالبق والذباب والزنبور والعقرب والسمك والضفدع والسرطان لا ينجسه) أي موت حيوان ليس له دم سائل في الماء القليل لا ينجسه. وقد جعل في الهداية هذه المسألة مسألتين فقال :أولاً : وموت ما ليس له نفس سائلة في الماء لا ينجسه كالبق والذباب والزنابير والعقرب ونحوها ثم قال : وموت ما يعيش في الماء لا يفسده كالسمك والضفدع والسرطان، وقد جمعهما قول المصنف «موت ما لا له» لأن مائي المولد دم له فكان الأنسب ما ذكره المصنف من حيث الاختصار إلا أنه يرد عليه ما كان مائي المولد دم له والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب .اهـ.
His saying: 'And the death of what has no blood in it like bedbugs, flies, hornets, scorpions, fish, frog, and crab does not contaminate it' means: The death of an animal that does not have flowing blood in a small amount of water does not contaminate it. And in Al-Hidayah, this issue was made into two issues: He said first: 'And the death of what does not have a flowing soul in water does not contaminate it, like bedbugs, flies, hornets, scorpions, and the like.' Then he said: 'And the death of what lives in water does not spoil it, like fish, frog, and crab.' And the author (of Kanz al-Daqa'iq) combined them in his statement 'death of what has no blood' because aquatic origin? Actually it has blood, so what the author mentioned was more appropriate in terms of brevity, except that it is objected that aquatic origin has blood, and Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling that when an animal without flowing blood (lā dam lahu) dies in water (even a small amount), it does not make the water najis. The author explains that the matn author (Ibn Nujaym) condensed two separate rules from Al-Hidayah into one phrase 'موت ما لا له' (death of what has no blood). Al-Hidayah lists two categories: (1) land animals with no flowing blood (like flies, scorpions) – their death does not contaminate water; (2) aquatic animals (like fish, frogs) – their death does not spoil the water. The matn combines them because both lack flowing blood. However, a critic objects that aquatic animals do have blood (e.g., fish have blood), so the combined phrase might be imprecise. The author notes this but leaves the matter to Allah's knowledge.

Key Terms
نفس سائلة (nafs sā'ilah): Flowing blood; the criterion for an animal's death to cause impurity in water.
مائي المولد (mā'ī al-mawlid): Aquatic origin; animals that live in water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a scorpion falls into a bucket of water and dies, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The water becomes impure because scorpions have blood
B) The water is pure and can be used for wudu
C) The water becomes impure only if the scorpion was dead before falling
D) The water becomes impure if the bucket is small

Key Takeaway
The death of animals without flowing blood (including land insects and aquatic animals) does not make water impure.

The Ruling on Urinating in Flowing vs. Stagnant Water
Intra-Hanafi dispute on urinating in water, resolved by distinguishing flowing vs. stagnant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi rulings for urinating in flowing versus stagnant water and explain the basis for the distinction.

Core Idea
Urinating in stagnant water is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi); in flowing water, only mildly disliked (makruh tanzihi).

This passage from Fatawa Qadikhan addresses an intra-Hanafi difference on whether urinating in water is prohibited. The key is distinguishing between flowing and stagnant water, with the heavier ruling for stagnant due to greater harm and stronger hadith evidence. Understanding this distinction clarifies how the school reconciles conflicting reports.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of water and reported positions
Note that the text distinguishes between flowing water (al-mā' al-jārī) and stagnant water (al-mā' al-rākid). For flowing, the correct view is dislike (karāhah). For stagnant, some say it is not haram (Abu al-Layth), others say it is haram.
The term 'makruh' in the flowing water context is later specified as tanzihi.
2
Examine the basis for the stricter view
The stricter view (stagnant water = haram) is based on a hadith. The text interprets this as karāhat al-tahrīm because the hadith's maximum implication is prohibitive dislike, not absolute prohibition.
This shows the Hanafi method of interpreting zanni dalil.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The text resolves the ikhtilāf by assigning different rulings to flowing and stagnant water: stagnant = tahrimi, flowing = tanzihi. This respects the hadith while avoiding contradiction with the consensus that it is not haram.
The reconciliation comes from Fatawa Qadikhan and al-Khabbazi's Hashiyat al-Hidayah.
4
Recognize the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position for fatwa: urinating in stagnant water is makruh tahrimi, in flowing water it is makruh tanzihi. This is the outcome of weighing evidence and applying the principle of differentiating based on harm.
A person who urinates in flowing water commits a minor dislike; in stagnant water, a more serious but not haram offense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : واختلفوا في كراهية البول في الماء الجاري والأصح هو الكراهة، وأما البول في الماء الراكد فقد نقل الشيخ جلال الدين الخبازي في حاشية الهداية عن أبي الليث أنه ليس بحرام إجماعاً بل مكروه، ونقل غيره أنه حرام ويحمل على كراهة التحريم لأن غاية ما يفيده الحديث كراهة التحريم فينبغي على هذا أن يكون البول في الماء الجاري مكروهاً كراهة تنزيه فرقاً بينه وبين البول في الماء الراكد.
And in Fatawa Qadikhan: They differed regarding the dislike of urinating in flowing water, and the most correct is dislike. As for urinating in stagnant water, Shaykh Jalal al-Din al-Khabbazi transmitted in Hashiyat al-Hidayah from Abu al-Layth that it is not haram by consensus but rather makruh. And others transmitted that it is haram, and it is interpreted as tahrimi dislike because the maximum the hadith indicates is tahrimi dislike. Based on this, urinating in flowing water should be tanzihi dislike, distinguishing between it and urinating in stagnant water.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about urinating in water. The source is Fatawa Qadikhan. The correct view (al-sahh) for flowing water is that it is disliked (makruh). For stagnant water, two positions are reported: from Abu al-Layth it is makruh (not haram by consensus), while others say it is haram. The text reconciles by classifying the stricter view as makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked) based on the hadith evidence. It then distinguishes between flowing water (makruh tanzihi) and stagnant water (makruh tahrimi).

Key Terms
الماء الجاري (al-mā' al-jārī): Flowing water (e.g., a river or stream); urinating in it is makruh tanzihi.
الماء الراكد (al-mā' al-rākid): Stagnant water (e.g., a pond or pool); urinating in it is makruh tahrimi.
كراهة التحريم (karāhat al-tahrīm): Prohibitive dislike; a level between haram and makruh tanzihi; requires avoiding but not as severe as haram.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school definitively differentiates between flowing water (makruh tanzihi) and stagnant water (makruh tahrimi) for the act of urinating, resolving internal disagreement by considering the degree of harm and hadith evidence.

Accepting a Muslim's Report on Water Impurity
Ruling on reliance upon a Muslim's testimony regarding water impurity and conditions for acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for accepting a Muslim's report on water impurity and explain the role of strong probability (ghalabat al-zann) in rulings of purity.

Core Idea
A just Muslim's report on impurity must be accepted; a fasiq's is rejected; for the unknown, two narrations exist.

In daily life, we often rely on others' information to determine purity of water. The Hanafi school sets clear rules: the reporter must be morally upright ('adl). A sinner's (fasiq) word is not taken. For those of unknown character (mustur), there is nuance. Additionally, when seeing animal footprints, strong suspicion (ghalabat al-zann) triggers impurity, not mere doubt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the reporter's moral status
Determine if the informant is 'adl (just), fasiq (sinner), or mustur (unknown). This determines whether his report is accepted.
The text clearly distinguishes these three categories.
2
Apply the ruling based on status
If 'adl: his report is accepted, and you cannot perform wudu with that water. If fasiq: his report is rejected. If mustur: consider the two narrations—some accept, some require investigation.
The mufta bihi for mustur may depend on later tarjih.
3
Understand the wild animal case
Seeing footprints of a wild animal near a small water source (well) does not automatically make it impure. It becomes impure only if you strongly suspect (ghalaba 'ala zannihi) that the animal drank from it.
This is based on the second branch mentioned in the passage.
4
Distinguish doubt from strong probability
Mere doubt (shakk) does not prevent wudu. The commentator clarifies that 'certainty' in the original text is interpreted as strong probability (ghalabat al-zann). This is crucial for daily practice.
This aligns with the principle that water remains pure until proven otherwise with strong evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى قاضيخان : إذا ورد الرجل ماء فأخبره مسلم أنه نجس لا يجوز له أن يتوضأ بذلك الماء . قالوا : هذا إذا كان عدلاً، فإن كان فاسقاً لا يصدق. وفي المستور روايتان وفي المبتغى بالغين المعجمة وبرؤية أثر أقدام الوحش عند الماء القليل لا يتوضأ به سبع مر بالركية وغلب على ظنه شربه منها تنجس وإلا فلا ا هـ . وينبغي أن يحمل الأول على ما إذا غلب على ظنه أن الوحش شربت منه بدليل الفرع الثاني وإلا فمجرد الشك لا يمنع الوضوء به بدليل ما قدمنا نقله عن الأصل أنه يتوضأ من الحوض الذي يخاف فيه قذراً ولا يتيقنه، وينبغي أن يحمل التيقن المذكور في الأصل من قوله ولا يتيقنه على غلبة الظن والخوف على الشك أو الوهم كما لا يخفى
And in Fatawa Qadikhan: If a man comes to water and a Muslim informs him that it is impure, he is not permitted to perform wudu with that water. They said: This is if the informant is 'adl (just). If he is fasiq (sinner), he is not believed. Regarding the mustur (one whose status is unknown), there are two narrations. And in al-Mubtagha (by the letter ghayn with point) and seeing the marks of wild animal's feet at a small amount of water, he does not perform wudu with it, seven times [??] by the well, and if he strongly suspects (ghalaba 'ala zannihi) that it drank from it, it becomes impure; otherwise not. And the first should be interpreted as when he strongly suspects that the animal drank from it, by evidence of the second branch; otherwise, mere doubt does not prevent wudu with it, by evidence of what we previously transmitted from al-Asl: one performs wudu from a basin in which he fears impurity but is not certain. And the certainty mentioned in al-Asl from his saying 'and he is not certain' should be interpreted as strong probability (ghalabat al-zann) and fear, not as doubt or delusion, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage from Fatawa Qadikhan outlines the rules for accepting information about water impurity. The key principle: a just ('adl) Muslim's report must be accepted and wudu cannot be performed with that water. A fasiq's report is rejected. For a person of unknown reliability (mustur), there are two narrations. The passage then discusses the case of seeing wild animal footprints near a well: if one strongly suspects the animal drank from it, the water becomes impure; otherwise mere doubt does not affect purity. The commentator clarifies that 'certainty' (yaqin) in the original text is understood as strong probability (ghalabat al-zann), not actual certainty.

Key Terms
عدل (‘adl): A Muslim of upright character whose testimony about impurity is accepted.
فاسق (fāsiq): A person who commits major sins or persists in minor sins; his report on impurity is not accepted.
مستور (mustūr): A person whose moral status is unknown; there are two narrations regarding acceptance of his report.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The acceptance of a report on water impurity depends on the reporter's moral character; in the absence of certainty, strong probability (ghalabat al-zann) is the operative threshold.

Dead Creatures Without Blood Do Not Impure Water
Ruling with proof from hadith, including intra- and cross-madhhab discussions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dead creatures without flowable blood in water and apply the hadith of the fly to analogous cases.

Core Idea
Dead creatures without flowing blood do not impurity water, as proven by the hadith of the fly.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that certain dead creatures—like flies, scorpions, and fish—do not render water impure when they die in it. The text from the commentary elaborates on two categories of creatures and defends the ruling with a clear hadith proof, addressing objections and a minor intra-madhhab difference. By the end, you will understand the legal reasoning and be able to apply it to similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and Its Categories
The ruling states that the death of creatures without flowable blood (e.g., bugs, flies) and aquatic animals (e.g., fish, frog) does not impurify water. Note that the matn combines two distinct categories under one phrase.
The commentator explains that Al-Hidayah treats them separately to avoid confusion about aquatic creatures that may have blood.
2
Analyze the Proof from Hadith
The primary proof is the hadith commanding immersion of a fly that falls into food or drink. Even if the fly dies, the food is not spoiled. This establishes the principle that creatures without flowing blood do not cause impurity.
The hadith is narrated by Bukhari, Nasa'i, and Ibn Majah. Its indication (wajh al-dalalah) is that the command to dip implies no spoilage.
3
Examine the Intra-Madhhab Difference
There is a difference within the Hanafi school: Abu Yusuf reportedly held that water-born creatures with flowing blood might impurify, but the relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that they do not.
The commentator cites Shams al-A'imma to resolve the objection, affirming the mufta bihi ruling.
4
Review the Cross-Madhhab Dispute
Imam al-Shafi'i's old view allegedly disagreed, but the text argues the correct Shafi'i position matches the Hanafi view, citing early consensus and Tabi'i opinions.
The thamarah al-khilaf: in practice, a fly in a well does not require removing water; Shafi'is following the old view might consider it impure.
5
Apply the Ruling to Analogous Creatures
The ruling extends to mosquitoes, locusts, beetles, bees, ants, fleas, lice, etc., either by direct implication (dalalat al-nass) or by consensus.
The commentator lists many examples, emphasizing that the criterion is absence of flowable blood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وموت ما لا دم له فيه كالبق والذباب والزنبور والعقرب والسمك والضفدع والسرطان لا ينجسه) أي موت حيوان ليس له دم سائل في الماء القليل لا ينجسه. وقد جعل في الهداية هذه المسألة مسألتين فقال :أولاً : وموت ما ليس له نفس سائلة في الماء لا ينجسه كالبق والذباب والزنابير والعقرب ونحوها ثم قال : وموت ما يعيش في الماء لا يفسده كالسمك والضفدع والسرطان، وقد جمعهما قول المصنف «موت ما لا له» لأن مائي المولد دم له فكان الأنسب ما ذكره المصنف من حيث الاختصار إلا أنه يرد عليه ما كان مائي المولد والممعاش وله دم سائل فإنه سيأتي أنه لا ينجس في ظاهر الرواية مع أن عبارة المصنف بخلافه فلذا فرق في الهداية بينهما، ونقل في الهداية خلاف الشافعي في المسألة الأولى وكذا في الثانية إلا في السمك، وما ذكره من خلاف الشافعي في الأولى ضعيف، والصحيح من مذهبه أنه كقولنا كما صرح به النووي في شرح المهذب وفي غاية البيان قال أبو الحسن الكرخي في شرح الجامع الصغير : لا أعلم أن فيه خلافاً بين الفقهاء ممن تقدم الشافعي، وإذا حصل الإجماع في الصدر الأوّل صار حجة على من بعده ا هـ .. وقد علمت أنه موافق لغيره وعلى تقدير مخالفته لا يكون خارقاً للإجماع فقد قال بقوله القديم يحيى بن أبي كثير التابعي الجليل كما نقله الخطابي ومحمد بن المنكدر الإمام التابعي كما نقله النووي، والدليل على أصل المسألة ما رواه البخاري في صحيحه بإسناده إلى أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه أنه قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم إذا وقع الذباب في إناء أحدكم فليغمسه ثم لينزعه فإن في أحد جناحيه داء وفي الآخر شفاء» (۱) وفي رواية النسائي وابن ماجه من حديث أبي سعيد الخدري «فإذا وقع في الطعام فامقلوه فيه فإنه يقدم السم ويؤخر الشفاء»(۱) ومعنى امقلوه أغمسوه. وجه الاستدلال به أن الطعام قد يكون حاراً فيموت بالغمس فيه، فلو كان يفسده لما أمر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بغمسه ليكون شفاء لنا إذا أكلناه وإذا ثبت الحكم في الذباب ثبت في غيره مما هو بمعناه كالبق والزنابير والعقرب والبعوض والجراد والخنفساء والنحل والنمل والصرصر والجعلان وبنات وردان والبرغوث والقمل. إما بدلالة النص أو بالاجماع كذا في المعراج . قال الإمام الخطابي : وقد تكلم على هذا الحديث من لا خلاق له وقال : كيف يجتمع الداء والشفاء في جناحي الذبابة، وكيف تعلم ذلك حتى تقدم جناح الداء قال : وهذا سؤال جاهل أو متجاهل، والذي يجد نفسه ونفوس عامة الحيوان قد جمع فيها الحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة وهي أشياء متضادة إذا تلاقت تفاسدت، ثم يرى الله عز وجل قد ألف بينها وجعلها سبباً لبقاء الحيوان وصلاحه الجدير أن لا ينكر اجتماع الداء والدواء في جزأين من حيوان واحد، وأن الذي ألهم النحلة اتخاذ بيت عجيب الصنعة وتعسل فيه، وألهم النملة كسب قوتها وادخاره لأوان حاجتها إليه وهو الذي خلق الذبابة وجعل لها الهداية إلى أن تقدم جناحاً وتؤخر آخر لما أراد الله من الابتلاء الذي هو مدرجة التعبد والامتحان الذي هو (ثم) والمعاش وله دم سائل الإيراد بناء على ظاهر ما سيأتي عن أبي يوسف رحمه الله حيث يفيد أن مائي المولد قد يكون له دم سائل وأما على ما قدمه آنفاً وما سيأتي عن شمس الأئمة فلا ورود.
His saying: (And the death of that which has no blood in it, like bugs, flies, hornets, scorpions, fish, frog, and crab does not make it impure) meaning: the death of an animal that does not have flowing blood in a small amount of water does not make it impure. Al-Hidayah made this two issues: first: the death of what does not have a flowing soul in water does not make it impure, like bugs, flies, hornets, scorpions, etc. Then he said: the death of what lives in water does not spoil it, like fish, frog, and crab. Both are combined by the author's statement 'death of that which has no' because water-born creatures have blood, so the author's wording was more appropriate for brevity except that it is objected to concerning what is water-born and lives [in water] and has flowing blood, for it will come that it does not make impure in the apparent narration while the author's wording is contrary to it, so al-Hidayah differentiated between them. And al-Hidayah transmitted the disagreement of al-Shafi'i in the first issue and similarly in the second except for fish. What he mentioned of al-Shafi'i's disagreement in the first is weak, and the correct view of his school is that it is like our statement, as al-Nawawi clarified in Sharh al-Muhadhdhab and in Ghayat al-Bayan. Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi said in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'I do not know that there is disagreement among the jurists before al-Shafi'i.' When consensus is established in the early generation, it becomes a proof against those after. And you have known that he agrees with others, and assuming he disagreed it would not break consensus, for Yahya ibn Abi Kathir, the great Tabi'i, agreed with his old view as al-Khattabi transmitted, and Muhammad ibn al-Munkadir, the imam Tabi'i, as al-Nawawi transmitted. The proof for the original issue is what al-Bukhari narrated in his Sahih with his chain to Abu Hurayra that the Messenger of Allah said: 'If a fly falls into the vessel of one of you, let him dip it then remove it, for in one of its wings is disease and in the other is cure.' And in the narration of al-Nasa'i and Ibn Majah from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri: 'If it falls into food, then dip it, for it presents the poison and delays the cure.' The meaning of 'imqaluhu' is 'dip it'. The indication from it is that food may be hot and the fly dies by dipping into it. If it spoiled it, the Prophet would not have commanded dipping it to be a cure for us when we eat it. And when the ruling is established for the fly, it is established for others like it: bugs, hornets, scorpions, mosquitoes, locusts, beetles, bees, ants, crickets, scarabs, bedbugs, fleas, and lice, either by implication of the text or by consensus, as in al-Mi'raj. Imam al-Khattabi said: 'One without moral goodness spoke about this hadith and said: How can disease and cure be combined in the two wings of a fly, and how does it know that so it presents the wing of disease?' He said: 'This is a question of an ignorant or pretentious person. One who sees that heat, cold, moisture, and dryness are gathered in himself and in the souls of all animals — things that are opposite and if they meet they corrupt — then he sees that Allah has harmonized them and made them a cause for the survival and well-being of the animal, he should not deny the combination of disease and remedy in two parts of one animal. And He who inspired the bee to make a wondrous house and to produce honey, and inspired the ant to earn its food and store it for its season of need, is the One who created the fly and gave it guidance to present one wing and delay the other for what Allah willed of trial which is a ladder of worship and an examination which is.' Then: And the water-born and lives [in water] and has flowing blood: the objection is based on what will come from Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, where it indicates that water-born creatures may have flowing blood, but based on what he presented earlier and what will come from Shams al-A'imma, there is no objection.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the death of creatures without blood in water. The author clarifies that the matn covers two distinct categories under one phrase: (1) land creatures without flowable blood (like bugs, flies, scorpions) and (2) aquatic creatures (fish, frog, crab). Al-Hidayah treats them separately, noting that some water-born animals may have blood, but the relied-upon (Zahir al-Riwayah) is that they still do not impurify water. The text then discusses the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, citing that the early consensus (including Tabi'is like Yahya ibn Abi Kathir) supports the Hanafi position. The proof comes from the hadith of the fly: the Prophet commanded dipping the entire fly if it falls into food/drink, implying it does not spoil the substance. The commentator expands by listing analogous creatures and refuting philosophical objections about the combination of disease and cure. Finally, the objection regarding water-born creatures with blood is dismissed based on Abu Yusuf's and Shams al-A'imma's views.

Key Terms
لا دم له (lā dam lah): Creatures without flowable (sā'il) blood; their death does not cause impurity in water.
مائي المولد (mā'ī al-mawlid): Creatures born in water (aquatic); their blood status is debated but they do not impurify water in Zahir al-Riwayah.
نفس سائلة (nafs sā'ila): Flowing blood; the criterion for whether the death of an animal in water causes impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that dead creatures without flowable blood do not impurity water, based on the hadith of the fly and early consensus, and this ruling is mufta bihi.

The Hadith of Salman: Proof for Purity of Creatures Without Blood
Proof from hadith for the ruling of animals without blood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on creatures without blood falling into liquids and explain the hadith evidence used to support it.

Core Idea
The hadith of Salman proves that creatures without blood are pure upon death.

In this lesson, we examine a key hadith used by the Hanafi school to establish the purity of liquids when a small creature without blood dies in them. The passage presents the hadith and then addresses criticisms of its chain, ultimately defending its reliability as a valid proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the hadith: what does it permit? Eating, drinking, and using the liquid for wudu. This indicates the liquid remains pure (tahir) and permissible (halal).
Focus on the word 'حلال' and 'وضوءه'.
2
Recognize the proof
The hadith is from Salman (ra) narrated by al-Daraqutni and Ibn 'Adi. Understand that this is a dalil naqli (textual evidence).
This is an ahad hadith, so its thubut is zanni.
3
Understand the challenge
Al-Daraqutni and Ibn 'Adi criticized the chain, claiming Baqiyyah is weak and Sa'id is unknown. This challenges the hadith's acceptability as proof.
The criticism is about the narrators' reliability.
4
Follow the Hanafi response
The author of Fath al-Qadir rebuts: Baqiyyah is Ibn al-Walid, a trustworthy narrator praised by Shu'bah; Sa'id is declared trustworthy by al-Khatib. Thus the hadith is at least hasan.
The defense relies on external testimonies of reliability.
5
Conclude the proof
With the chain defended, the hadith stands as valid evidence. The ruling is established: liquids remain pure when a creature without blood dies in them.
This proof supports the Hanafi position that only flowing blood (dam masfuh) causes impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستدل مشايخنا أيضاً على أصل المسألة بما عن سلمان رضي الله عنه عنه عليه السلام قال : يا سلمان كل طعام وشراب وقعت فيه دابة ليس لها دم فماتت فهي فهو حلال أكله وشربه ووضوءه. قال الزيعلي رحمه الله تعالى : المخرج رواه الدارقطني. وقال : لم يروه إلا بقية عن سعيد بن أبي سعيد الزبيدي وهو ضعيف ورواه ابن عدي في الكامل وأعله هذا وقال : هو شيخ مجهول وحديثه غير محفوظ ا هـ. قال العلامة في فتح القدير : ودفعاً بأن بقية هذا هو ابن الوليد روى عنه الأئمة مثل الحمادين وابن المبارك ويزيد بن هرون وابن عيينة ووكيع والأوزاعي واسحق بن راهويه وشعبة وناهيك بشعبة واحتياطه. قال يحيى : كان شعبة ميجلاً لبقية حيث قدم بغداد وقد روى له الجماعة إلا البخاري. وأما سعيد بن أبي سعيد هذا فذكره الخطيب وقال واسم أبيه عبد الجبار وكان ثقة فانتفت الجهالة والحديث مع هذا لا ينزل عن الحسن ا.هـ.
And our shuyukh also argued for the original issue based on what was reported from Salman (may Allah be pleased with him) from the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'O Salman, every food or drink in which falls a small creature that has no blood and dies, then it is permissible to eat it, drink it, and perform wudu with it.' Al-Zayla'i (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The source is that al-Daraqutni narrated it. And he said: Only Baqiyyah narrated it from Sa'id ibn Abi Sa'id al-Zubaidi, and he is weak. Ibn 'Adi narrated it in al-Kamil and declared it defective, saying: He is an unknown shaykh and his hadith is not preserved, end quote. The 'Allamah in Fath al-Qadir said: Rebutting [this] by saying that Baqiyyah here is Ibn al-Walid; many imams narrated from him such as al-Hammad, Ibn al-Mubarak, Yazid ibn Harun, Ibn 'Uyaynah, Waki', al-Awza'i, Ishaq ibn Rahwayh, Shu'bah—and Shu'bah is sufficient for his caution. Yahya said: Shu'bah used to hold Baqiyyah in high esteem when he came to Baghdad. All the [hadith] collections except al-Bukhari have narrated from him. As for Sa'id ibn Abi Sa'id, al-Khatib mentioned him and said his father's name was 'Abd al-Jabbar and he was trustworthy, so the unknown status is removed, and the hadith with this is not less than hasan.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi proof from hadith for the ruling that creatures without blood (like flies, bees) do not impurify food or water when they die in them. The hadith from Salman is cited, but its chain was criticized by al-Daraqutni and Ibn 'Adi. The author of Fath al-Qadir refutes this criticism by demonstrating that both narrators, Baqiyyah and Sa'id, are reliable according to leading hadith critics. Baqiyyah Ibn al-Walid is a trustworthy narrator from whom many great imams narrated, and Shu'bah held him in high regard. Sa'id ibn Abi Sa'id is declared trustworthy by al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, so the hadith is at least hasan (good) in strength. Thus the Hanafi school finds this proof sufficient.

Key Terms
دابة (dābbah): A small creeping creature, insect, or animal without flowing blood.
دم (dam): Flowing blood that is impure when separated from the body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith of Salman (ra), what is the ruling on a creature without blood that falls into water and dies?
A) The water becomes impure and cannot be used for wudu.
B) The water remains pure and is permissible for wudu and drinking.
C) The water becomes makruh (disliked) but still valid for wudu.
D) It depends on the creature's size.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that creatures without flowing blood do not impurify liquids, relying on the hadith of Salman which they consider at least hasan.

Blood Impurity and the Leech Case: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi analysis of blood impurity applied to leech case

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that only flowing blood (dam masfūḥ) is impure and explain how this applies to the case of a leech that sucks blood, including the difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad and the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
Only flowing blood impurities; blood in its vessel is not impure – even in a leech's stomach.

You might assume any blood contact makes water impure. The Hanafi school draws a critical distinction: blood that remains in its natural 'mine' (such as inside a living creature) is not impure. This lesson examines a classical intra-Hanafi dispute about a leech that falls into water after sucking blood, and the settled ruling that the water becomes spoiled but not najis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The passage begins by establishing that the impurifier is flowing blood (al-dam al-masfūḥ) that mixes with the animal's parts at death. Blood that stays inside living animals (in its vessel) is not impure.
This principle is supported by the analogy with mud: prohibition does not entail impurity.
2
Understand the objections and answers
Two objections are raised: (1) the slaughtered animal of a Magian or one who omits the name is impure despite removal of flowing blood, and (2) a Muslim's slaughter where blood did not flow due to a hindrance is pure. The answers rely on the Lawgiver's ruling regarding eligibility and what is within human power.
These objections reinforce that the principle is not absolute but subject to additional rulings.
3
Focus on the leech case
The text turns to a bug (leech) that sucks blood and falls into water. Abu Yusuf argues the water remains pure because the blood is 'borrowed' (musta‘ār) still in its vessel (the bug's stomach). Muhammad argues the water becomes impure because the blood has left its original vessel.
The difference hinges on whether the bug's stomach is considered a continuation of the blood's original vessel.
4
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
The relied-upon view (al-ashahh) is that the leech spoils the water (yufsid al-mā') without making it najis. The water becomes makruh tahrimi to use.
This view is attributed to al-Mujtaba and is the mufta bihi.
5
Extend the ruling
The author extends the same ruling to ticks and fleas that suck blood: they spoil the water if they fall in after sucking blood.
This practical extension shows how the principle is applied to similar cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في الهداية ولأن المنجس اختلاط الدم المسفوح بأجزائه عند الموت حتى حل المذكي لانعدام الدم فيه ولا دم فيها والحرمة ليست من ضرورتها النجاسة كالطين وأورد عليه ذبيحة المجوسي ومتروك التسمية عامداً فإنها نجسة مع زوال الدم المسفوح، وذبيحة المسلم إذا لم يسل منها الدم لعارض بأن أكلت ورق العناب فإنها حلال أن الدم لم يسل، وأجاب الأكمل وغيره عن الأول بأن القياس الطهارة كالمسلم إلا أن صاحب الشرع أخرجه عن أهلية الذبح فذبحه كلا ذبح. وعن الثاني أن الشارع أقام الأهلية واستعمال آلة الذبح مقام الإسالة لإتيانه بما هو داخل تحت قدرته ولا يعتبر بالعوارض لأنها لا تدخل تحت القواعد الأصلية وأجاب في معراج الدراية بأن ذبيحة المجوسي والوثني وتارك التسمية عمداً طاهر على الأصح وإن لم تؤكل لعدم أهلية الذابح، وعزاه إلى المجتبى ثم قال : فإن قيل لو كان المنجس هو الدم يلزم أن يكون الدموي من الحيوان نجساً، سواء كان قبل الحياة أو بعدها لأنه يشمل على الدم في كلتا الحالتين. قلنا : الدم حال الحياة في معدنه والدم في معدنه لا يكون نجساً بخلاف الذي بعد الموت لأن الدماء بعد الموت تنصب عن مجاريها فلا تبقى في معادنها فيتنجس اللحم بتشربه إياها ولهذا لو قطعت العروق بعد الموت لا يسيل الدم منها وفي صلاة :البقالي : لو مص البق الدم لم ينجس عند أبي يوسف لأنه مستعار، وعند محمد ينجسه وفي جمع الخلاف على العكس. والأصح في العلق إذا مص الدم أنه يفسد الماء، قال صاحب المجتبى : ومن هذا يعرف حكم القراد والحلم ا هـ .
It is stated in al-Hidayah: 'Because the impurifier is the mixing of flowing blood with its parts at the time of death, so that [the meat of] an animal slaughtered legally is made permissible due to the absence of blood in it, and these [creatures] have no blood; prohibition does not necessitate impurity, like mud.' An objection was raised concerning the slaughtered animal of a Magian and one who deliberately omits the name [of Allah]—that it is impure despite the removal of flowing blood; and concerning the slaughtered animal of a Muslim from which blood did not flow due to a hindrance, such as eating grapes, that it is permissible while blood did not flow. Al-Akmal and others answered the first: analogy would require purity like [the animal of] a Muslim, but the Lawgiver excluded it from eligibility for slaughter, so its slaughter is like no slaughter. Regarding the second, the Lawgiver treated eligibility and use of slaughtering tools as equivalent to the flowing [of blood] because the person did what is within his power; temporary hindrances are not considered because they are not subject to established principles. In Mi'raj al-Dirayah, it is answered that the slaughtered animal of a Magian, an idolater, or one who deliberately omits the name is pure according to the most correct view, though not eaten, due to the lack of eligibility of the slaughterer, and this is attributed to al-Mujtaba. Then he said: If it is said: if the impurifier is blood, then it would follow that blood in an animal's body is impure, both before and after life, because it contains blood in both states, we say: blood during life is in its vessel, and blood in its vessel is not impure, unlike after death, because after death blood drains from its channels, so it does not remain in its vessels, thus the meat becomes impure by absorbing it. This is why if the veins are cut after death, blood does not flow from them. In the chapter: al-Baqqali: if a bug sucks blood, it does not become impure according to Abu Yusuf because it is borrowed, while according to Muhammad it becomes impure; and in Jam' al-Khilaf it is the opposite. The most correct concerning the leech: if it sucks blood, it spoils the water. The author of al-Mujtaba said: from this is known the ruling for ticks and fleas, end quote.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage first establishes the general principle that impurity arises from the mixing of flowing blood with the animal's parts at death. The Hanafi school then addresses two objections: (1) why the meat of a Magian's slaughter is impure despite blood flow, and (2) why a Muslim's slaughter where blood did not flow (due to abnormal cause) is considered permissible. The answers rely on the Lawgiver's ruling on eligibility and on the concept of what is within human power. Then the text focuses on the specific case of a bug (leech) that sucks blood from a person and then falls into water. Abu Yusuf holds it does not impurify the water because the blood is 'borrowed' and still in its natural vessel (the bug's body). Muhammad holds it does impurify the water. The preferred view (al-ashahh) is that a leech sucking blood renders the water corrupt (yufsid al-ma'). The author extends the same ruling to ticks and fleas that suck blood: they spoil the water if they fall in after sucking blood.

Key Terms
الدم المسفوح (al-dam al-masfūḥ): Blood that flows out when an animal is slaughtered or injured.
العلق (al-‘alaq): A leech that sucks blood from humans or animals.
مستعار (musta‘ār): Borrowed; Abu Yusuf's term meaning the blood in the bug's stomach is not yet separated from its original 'mine'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling of water into which a leech has fallen after sucking blood?
A) The water remains pure and permissible.
B) The water becomes impure (najis).
C) The water is spoiled (fāsid) and makruh tahrimi to use.
D) The water is halal as long as the leech is small.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that only flowing blood (dam masfūḥ) is impure; blood in its vessel (even inside a leech) does not make water najis, but the water becomes spoiled (fāsid) and impermissible for use.
